Computer Assocs. Int'l v. Altai, Inc.


No. 94-0433


SUPREME COURT OF TEXAS


918 S.W.2d 453; 1996 Tex. LEXIS 29; 39 Tex. Sup. J. 422; 11 I.E.R. Cas. (BNA) 923


September 22, 1994, Argued  
March 14, 1996, Delivered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As Amended March 27, 1996.


PRIOR HISTORY: ON CERTIFIED QUESTION FROM THE UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE SECOND CIRCUIT.

This Opinion Substituted on Overrule of Motion for Rehearing for Withdrawn Opinion of June 8, 1995, Previously Reported at: 1995 Tex. LEXIS 79.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant computer company sought review of an order from the United States Court of Appeals for the Second Circuit (Texas), holding that the statute of limitations barred an action by appellee developer of computer software for misappropriation of trade secrets. The case was before the court on certified questions.


OVERVIEW: Appellee computer software developer left his position with appellant computer company to work for appellee computer company. The employee took copies of a computer source code from appellant and used them in his new position. Appellant did not discover that the employee had copied and made use of the code until four years after he resigned. Appellant sued appellee for misappropriation of trade secrets. The trial court determined that the discovery rule exception to Tex. Civ. Prac. & Rem. Code Ann. § 16.003(a) did not apply and that appellant's action for misappropriation of trade secrets was barred by a two-year statute of limitations. The court of appeals certified to this court two questions: does the discovery rule exception to § 16.003(a) apply; and if not, whether § 16.003(a) violated the "open courts" provision of the Texas Constitution. The court held that the nature of trade secret property rights, which required an owner to vigilantly guard the secret from the world in order to preserve its rights, made application of the two-year statute of limitations reasonable under the circumstances. Thus, the discovery rule exception did not apply and § 16.003(a) was constitutional.


OUTCOME: Answering the certified questions, the court held that the discovery rule exception did not apply to the claim for misappropriation of trade secrets and that application of the applicable statute of limitations provision did not violate the Texas Constitution.

Baptist Mem. Hosp. Sys. v. Arredondo


No. 95-0861


SUPREME COURT OF TEXAS


922 S.W.2d 120; 1996 Tex. LEXIS 14; 39 Tex. Sup. J. 286



February 9, 1996, Delivered


PRIOR HISTORY:   [**1] ON APPLICATION FOR WRIT OF ERROR TO THE COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE FOURTH DISTRICT OF TEXAS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner health care providers sought review of the decision of the Court of Appeals for the Fourth District of Texas that found that the statute of limitations, Tex. Rev. Civ. Stat. Ann. art. 4590i, § 10.01, did not bar the wrongful death action filed by respondent mother.


OVERVIEW: Respondent mother filed a wrongful death action against petitioner health care providers. On appeal, the court reversed the lower court's decision, finding instead that the statute of limitations barred respondent's wrongful death claim. The court held that a wrongful death claim based on medical malpractice was controlled by the Medical Liability and Insurance Improvement Act, and must have been filed within two years of the alleged negligent act, pursuant to Tex. Rev. Civ. Stat. Ann. art. 4590i, § 10.01. The court held that the tolling provision of § 10.01 applied to claims filed by a minor or on a minor's behalf, but that the tolling provision did not apply to the wrongful death claim filed by respondent, who was an adult. The court found that § 10.01 did not violate the open courts provision of Tex. Const. art. I, § 13. The court also held that § 10.01 was not unconstitutional under Tex. Const. art. I, § 19. Finally, the court held that the fraudulent concealment defense did not toll the statute of limitations because respondent did not establish that petitioners had actual knowledge of any wrong or negligence or that they had a fixed purpose to conceal any wrong or negligence.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the lower court because the statute of limitations barred the wrongful death claim filed by respondent mother against petitioner health care providers. Respondent's claim was filed more then two years after the alleged negligent act, the statute of limitations was not tolled because respondent was an adult beneficiary, and the statute of limitations was not unconstitutional.

Chenault v. Phillips


No. 95-0865


SUPREME COURT OF TEXAS


914 S.W.2d 140; 1996 Tex. LEXIS 1; 39 Tex. Sup. J. 204



January 11, 1996, Delivered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Relators filed an original petition seeking a declaration that the Attorney Occupation Tax, Texas Tax Code §§ 191.141-191.145, was unconstitutional, and they brought a petition for a writ of mandamus.


OVERVIEW: Seeking a declaration that the Attorney Occupation Tax, Texas Tax Code §§ 191.141-191.145 was unconstitutional, relators filed an original petition with the court. The court held that a request for declaratory relief did not establish jurisdiction in the court. Thus, the court held that it could not consider the merits of relators' request. Relators also brought a writ of mandamus. The court held that mandamus was an extraordinary remedy that was not available from any state court when a party had an adequate legal remedy. The court found that relators had not met their burden of showing that pursuing their claims first in the trial court would cause them to suffer any immediate harm entitling them to mandamus relief. Moreover, the court declared that it would not issue an original writ of mandamus absent a compelling reason. Relators asserted as compelling reasons for the invocation of the court's original jurisdiction the need to protect the court's role as the ultimate supervisory authority over the state bar. The court held that the court's inherent powers, such as the power to regulate the bar, were administrative powers, not jurisdictional powers.


OUTCOME: The court overruled relators' motion for leave to file an original petition for writ of mandamus and relators' original petition seeking declaratory relief. The court held that the court did not have jurisdiction to hear a request for declaratory relief. Also, the court held that relators failed to meet their burden demonstrating that they were entitled to mandamus relief.

Barber v. Colorado Indep. Sch. Dist.


No. 94-0054


SUPREME COURT OF TEXAS


901 S.W.2d 447; 1995 Tex. LEXIS 111; 38 Tex. Sup. J. 902; 58 A.L.R.5th 799


November 16, 1994, Argued  
June 22, 1995, Delivered


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] ON APPLICATION FOR WRIT OF ERROR TO THE COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE ELEVENTH DISTRICT OF TEXAS.



DISPOSITION: For all of these reasons, we affirm the judgment of the court of appeals.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners sought review of a judgment of the Court of Appeals for the Eleventh District (Texas) reversing a decision by the trial court that respondent's grooming code, which imposed hair length and erring restrictions on male high school students, violated the state Constitution.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners filed a class action challenging the legality under the state Constitution of hair length and earring restrictions imposed by respondent upon its male high school students. The trial court held that respondent's grooming code violated the state Constitution, and granted a permanent injunction against respondent, prohibiting enforcement of the regulations. The appellate court reversed, holding that judicial intervention was inappropriate in the case. The Supreme Court refused to use the state Constitution to micro-manage state high schools. The court stated that although minors had constitutional rights, they did not have the same constitutional rights as adults. Consequently, the court held that the state had more control over the conduct of minors than it did over adults. Therefore, the judgment of the appellate court was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed because respondent had the authority to impose hair length and earring restrictions upon its male high school students. The court refused to use the state Constitution to micro-manage state high schools.

Krishnan v. Sepulveda


No. D-3173


SUPREME COURT OF TEXAS


916 S.W.2d 478; 1995 Tex. LEXIS 97; 38 Tex. Sup. J. 806


October 12, 1993, Argued  
June 15, 1995, Delivered


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] ON APPLICATION FOR WRIT OF ERROR TO THE COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE THIRTEENTH DISTRICT OF TEXAS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondents sought review of an order of the trial court (Texas) dismissing their action, arguing that they were entitled to bring a wrongful death or survival action for the death of a stillborn fetus that was allegedly caused by petitioner's negligent medical treatment of the mother.


OVERVIEW: Respondents sued petitioner alleging that her negligence in providing diagnosis, prenatal supervision, and treatment of the mother caused their child to be delivered stillborn. The trial court sustained petitioner's special exception and dismissed the case. The appeals court reversed and remanded the case. Although the overwhelming majority of states now permit some form of recovery for the loss of a fetus, the court declined to overrule its prior opinions, and held that there was no wrongful death or survival cause of action for the death of a fetus. However, the court allowed the mother, and not the father, to recover mental anguish damages suffered as a result of her injury that was proximately caused by petitioner's negligence. Finally, respondents were not entitled to damages for their loss of society, companionship, and affection suffered as a result of the loss of the fetus.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed because appellants were not entitled to bring a wrongful death or survival action for the death of a stillborn fetus. The mother recovered mental anguish damages she suffered as a result of injuries she sustained that were proximately caused by petitioner's negligence.

Weiner v. Wasson


No. 94-0541


SUPREME COURT OF TEXAS


900 S.W.2d 316; 1995 Tex. LEXIS 81; 38 Tex. Sup. J. 750


January 5, 1995, Argued  
June 8, 1995, Delivered


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] ON APPLICATION FOR WRIT OF ERROR TO THE COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE TENTH DISTRICT OF TEXAS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant surgeon filed an application for writ of error from a decision by the Court of Appeals for the Tenth District (Texas), holding that the Medical Liability and Insurance Improvement Act, Tex. Rev. Civ. Stat. Ann. art. 4590i, § 10.01 (1994) was unconstitutional as applied to minors such as plaintiff in negligence actions for medical malpractice.


OVERVIEW: Applying for a writ of error, defendant surgeon challenged the appeals court's judgment declaring § 10.01 of the Medical Liability and Insurance Improvement Act (Act), Tex. Rev. Civ. Stat. Ann. art. 4590i, § 10.01 (1994), unconstitutional as applied to minors suing for personal injuries. When plaintiff was 15 years old, defendant operated on plaintiff's hip, causing bone injuries that required additional surgeries and, after plaintiff turned 18, a total hip replacement. Plaintiff sued for negligence. The trial court dismissed because the Act's two-year statute of limitations had expired. The court of appeals reversed. On appeal, the state's highest court agreed that the Act was unconstitutional as applied to minors under the "open courts" provision in Tex. Const. art. I, § 13, because the Act precluded plaintiff from suing after he reached his majority. The two-year statute of limitations for minors' personal injury claims in Tex. Civ. Prac. & Rem. Ann. §§ 16.001, 16.003, was tolled until plaintiff reached 18. Because plaintiff brought suit within two years after his 18th birthday, his suit was timely. The court affirmed the decision of the appeals court and remanded for trial.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the court of appeals' decision holding a section of a state medical liability act unconstitutional because its prohibition against minors bringing personal injury suits more than two years after injury impinged on plaintiff's state constitutional right to seek legal redress in the state's courts after he reached his majority. The court remanded for trial of plaintiff's negligence claims.

 

49 of 1793 DOCUMENTS


Corpus Christi People's Baptist Church v. Nueces County Appraisal Dist.


No. D-4333


SUPREME COURT OF TEXAS


904 S.W.2d 621; 1995 Tex. LEXIS 70; 38 Tex. Sup. J. 653


October 20, 1994, Argued  
May 25, 1995, DELIVERED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] ON APPLICATION FOR WRIT OF ERROR TO THE COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE THIRTEENTH DISTRICT OF TEXAS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner church challenged the judgment of the Court of Appeals for the 13th District of Texas, which held that the statute providing for a late application for a religious organization exemption from property taxes, Tex. Tax Code Ann. § 11.433, violated Tex. Const. art. III, § 55. The court of appeals did not reach respondent county's argument that the exemption violated Tex. Const. art. I, § 16.


OVERVIEW: Respondent county brought an action against petitioner church to collect unpaid taxes and denied petitioner's late application for a religious organization exemption from property taxes under Tex. Tax Code Ann. § 11.433 The court rejected respondent's argument that taxes were an obligation to a taxing entity that could not be forgiven under Tex. Const. art. III, § 55. The court held that §11.433 simply prescribed the time in which a tax exemption must be claimed and did not release or extinguish tax obligations. When an application was no longer allowed, the taxing unit was free to collect its taxes. The court compared the tolling provision in § 11.433 to statutes of limitations, which were permissible under § 55 because they did not release or extinguish a debt but merely affected the remedy when its enforcement was sought. The court also held that § 11.433 was not a retroactive law prohibited by Tex. Const. art. I, § 16. The court found that a taxing unit had no vested right to taxes until the exemption was determined and that § 11.433 did not extinguish any tax liability. The court reversed the judgment of the court of appeals and affirmed the judgment of the trial court.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the court of appeals and affirmed the judgment of the trial court because the statute providing for a late application for a religious organization exemption from property taxes did not violate the Texas constitution. The statute merely prescribed the time in which a tax exemption had to be claimed and did not release or extinguish an obligation to the state. The statute was not a prohibited retroactive law.

 

50 of 1793 DOCUMENTS


Thomas v. Oldham


No. D-4559 and No. 94-0207


SUPREME COURT OF TEXAS


895 S.W.2d 352; 1995 Tex. LEXIS 29; 38 Tex. Sup. J. 375


September 21, 1994, Argued  
March 16, 1995, Delivered


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] ON APPLICATION FOR WRIT OF ERROR TO THE COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE FOURTEENTH DISTRICT OF TEXAS. ON APPLICATION FOR WRIT OF ERROR TO THE COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE THIRTEENTH DISTRICT OF TEXAS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court granted writs of error to resolve a conflict between the Courts of Appeal for the Thirteenth and Fourteenth Districts (Texas) regarding whether Tex. Civ. Prac. & Rem. Code § 101.106 barred the simultaneous rendition of judgment against a governmental unit and its employee.


OVERVIEW: In each of two cases that were consolidated, a governmental employee injured a motorist. In the first case, the trial court rendered judgment against defendant employee and defendant city jointly and severally. The court of appeals held that the judgment against defendant city barred judgment against defendant employee under the Tort Claims Act, Tex. Civ. Prac. & Rem. Code § 101.106. In the second case, a different court of appeals held that the Act did not bar a concurrent judgment against a governmental unit and its employee. On review, the court held that a judgment against a governmental unit under the Act barred a simultaneous judgment against the employee whose actions gave rise to the claim. The statutory bar applied if the judgment against the governmental unit occurred at any time before or during the pendency of a suit against an employee. Although a party who pursued the statutory remedy against a governmental unit could lose her common law remedy against the employee, the party could also choose to pursue the common law remedy and forego or postpone any attempt to collect from the government. The Act thus did not unconstitutionally restrict a common law right of action.


OUTCOME: The court held that a judgment against a governmental unit under the Tort Claims Act barred a simultaneous judgment against a governmental employee whose actions gave rise to the claim. Because the judgments against defendant cities were rendered during the time in which the trial court had plenary power over the actions against defendant employees, the latter actions were statutorily barred.

 

51 of 1793 DOCUMENTS


Travelers Indem. Co. v. Fuller


No. 94-0648


SUPREME COURT OF TEXAS


892 S.W.2d 848; 1995 Tex. LEXIS 6; 38 Tex. Sup. J. 298



February 16, 1995, Delivered


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] ON APPLICATION FOR WRIT OF ERROR TO THE COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE NINTH DISTRICT OF TEXAS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner insurer sought review of the decision of the Court of Appeals for the Ninth District of Texas, which found that respondent heir did have a cause of action under Tex. Const. art. XVI for punitive damages against petitioner due to the death of respondent's father.


OVERVIEW: Respondent heir filed a claim against petitioner insurer alleging gross negligence by petitioner for its failure to alleviate health and safety hazards at respondent's father's place of employment. Petitioner filed a motion for summary judgment and asserted immunity due to the Texas Workers' Compensation Act, Tex. Lab. Code Ann. § 411.003(a) (1995). The trial court granted summary judgment in favor of petitioner, but the court of appeals reversed. The supreme court reversed the court of appeals. Respondent contended that Tex. Const. art. XVI, § 26 (1993) provided a punitive damages cause of action. However, the supreme court examined the legal history and concluded that art. XVI, § 26 was intended to permit heirs to pursue punitive damages where a statutory cause of action for compensatory damages existed, but did not grant an independent punitive recovery. Respondent also asserted that the Texas Open Courts Provision, Tex. Const. art. I, § 13 was violated by § 411.003(a), but the court held that the open courts provision did not apply because respondent's cause of action did not exist at common law.


OUTCOME: The supreme court reversed the court of appeals' decision that respondent heir had a cause of action for punitive damages against petitioner insurer because constitutional provision was not intended to provide a new cause of action for punitive damages but only to permit punitive damages based on compensatory damages.

 

52 of 1793 DOCUMENTS


City of Beaumont v. Bouillion


No. D-4004


SUPREME COURT OF TEXAS


896 S.W.2d 143; 1995 Tex. LEXIS 13; 38 Tex. Sup. J. 282; 10 I.E.R. Cas. (BNA) 548; 130 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P57,883


May 4, 1994, Argued  
February 16, 1995, Delivered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As Corrected.


PRIOR HISTORY: ON APPLICATION FOR WRIT OF ERROR TO THE COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE NINTH DISTRICT OF TEXAS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, city and officials, sought review of the decision of the Court of Appeals for the Ninth District of Texas, which affirmed the verdict in favor of appellees, former city employees. Appellees brought suit against appellants claiming constructive discharge and violations under the Texas Whistleblowers Act and Tex. Const. art. I, §§ 8, 27.


OVERVIEW: Appellee former city employees claimed that they were constructively discharged from their jobs by appellant city and officials for reporting official misconduct to an authority and for exercising their constitutional rights to free speech and free assembly. Appellees asserted violations of the Texas Whistleblowers Act and Tex. Const. art. I, §§ 8, 27. The trial court and the appellate court upheld a jury award for appellees. On review, the court reversed the judgment of the appellate court and rendered judgment for appellants. The court held that the Act was not implicated by appellees' reports to the press and that there was no report to an appropriate authority; thus, appellees' actions did not bring their complaint within the realm of the Act. The court found that appellees failed to relate to the court any basis for the Texas Constitution affording more than equitable relief for violations of its provisions. The court held that there was no private right of action for damages arising under the free speech and free assembly sections of art. I, §§ 8, 27. The court also held that Texas does not recognize a common law cause of action for damages to enforce constitutional rights.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the appellate court and rendered judgment that appellees, former city employees, take nothing. The court held the conduct of appellees fell outside the ambit of the Texas Whistleblowers Act and that there was no implied cause of action for damages against appellants, city and officials, for violations of the free speech and free assembly clauses of the Texas Constitution.

 

53 of 1793 DOCUMENTS


Texas Workers' Compensation Comm'n v. Garcia


No. D-4270


SUPREME COURT OF TEXAS


893 S.W.2d 504; 1995 Tex. LEXIS 5; 38 Tex. Sup. J. 235


Argued May 11, 1994  
February 9, 1995, Delivered


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] ON APPLICATION FOR WRIT OF ERROR TO THE COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE FOURTH DISTRICT OF TEXAS.



DISPOSITION: For the foregoing reasons, we reverse the judgment of the court of appeals and render judgment upholding the validity of the Act against plaintiffs' constitutional challenges.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, state and employers, appealed the judgment of the Court of Appeals for the Fourth District of Texas, that affirmed a judgment for plaintiffs, workers and unions, holding that the legislature violated the open courts, equal protection, due course of law, and trial by jury guarantees of the Texas Constitution by adopting an impairment-based system under the Texas Workers' Compensation Act, Tex. Lab. Code Ann. § 401.001 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, workers and unions, brought an action against defendants, state and employers, claiming that the Texas Workers' Compensation Act (Act), Tex. Lab. Code Ann. § 401.001 et seq., was unconstitutional. The court of appeals held that the Act's impairment-based system violated the Texas Constitution's open courts, equal protection, due course of law, and trial by jury guarantees. On appeal, the court reversed and rendered judgment upholding the Act's validity. The court held that the Act was an adequate substitute for common law negligence for purposes of the open courts guarantee as it provided benefits without the necessity of proving negligence; did not violate the equal protection guarantee because the Act's requirement that a claimant suffer at least a 15 percent impairment to be eligible for supplemental income benefits was rationally related to a legitimate state purpose; met constitutional due course requirements because the Act's use of impairment in general and the 15 percent impairment threshold for supplemental income benefits in particular was sufficiently rational and reasonable; and provided that relevant issues would be decided by a jury.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the court of appeals and rendered judgment upholding the constitutionality of the Texas Workers' Compensation Act because it did not facially violate the Texas Constitution's guarantees of open courts, equal protection, due course of law, or trial by jury. The Act was an adequate substitute for a common law remedy, its impairment-based system was rational, and relevant issues were decided by a jury.

 

54 of 1793 DOCUMENTS


Edgewood Indep. Sch. Dist. v. Meno


No. 94-0152


SUPREME COURT OF TEXAS


893 S.W.2d 450; 1995 Tex. LEXIS 4; 38 Tex. Sup. J. 188


May 25, 1994, ARGUED  
January 30, 1995, DELIVERED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As Corrected. Second Correction May 4, 1995. Modified Opinion Reported at: 1995 Tex. LEXIS 169.


PRIOR HISTORY: ON DIRECT APPEAL FROM THE 250TH JUDICIAL DISTRICT COURT OF TRAVIS COUNTY, TEXAS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The 250th Judicial District Court of Travis County (Texas), found 1993 Tex. Gen. Laws 1479, the statutory scheme for public school finance, constitutional but found the statute did not provide efficient funding for facilities and enjoined the issuance of bonds. Appellant school districts challenged the judgment that the statute was constitutional. Appellee, State of Texas, challenged the court's finding concerning funding for facilities.


OVERVIEW: In a challenge to the public school finance bill passed by the legislature as 1993 Tex. Gen. Laws 1479, the trial court held the system met the requirements of Tex. Const. art. VII, §1, but found that the legislature failed to provide efficiently for facilities and enjoined the issuance of bonds until the inefficiency was corrected. Appellants, nine groups of school districts, local officials, and parents, perfected direct appeals. Groups of appellants challenged the efficiency of the system, the revenue system, and the distribution of excess county education district funds. Another group complained of the dismissal of their cause of action. Appellee state perfected an appeal complaining of the trial court's ruling on facilities. The court held that efficiency did not require equal access to funding at all levels and the system provided a general diffusion of knowledge under the funding provided. The court affirmed the constitutionality of the public school finance system enacted. The court held that the challenge to the school finance law based on inadequate provision for facilities failed only because of an evidentiary void.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the constitutionality of the public school finance system enacted by the legislature because it provided for an efficient system for the support and maintenance of public schools for the general diffusion of knowledge. The court vacated the injunction against the issuance of bonds because it found that the funding provided under the statute efficiently provided for facilities.

 

55 of 1793 DOCUMENTS


Edgewood Indep. Sch. Dist. v. Meno


NO. 94-0152


SUPREME COURT OF TEXAS


917 S.W.2d 717; 1995 Tex. LEXIS 169


May 25, 1994, Argued  
January 30, 1995, Opinion Delivered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [*1] As Modified February 16, 1995.


PRIOR HISTORY: ON DIRECT APPEAL FROM THE 250TH JUDICIAL DISTRICT COURT OF TRAVIS COUNTY, TEXAS. Original Opinion Reported at: 1995 Tex. LEXIS 4.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, nine groups of school districts and parent intervenors, challenged a judgment of the 250th Judicial District Court of Travis County, Texas, holding that Senate Bill 7, 1993 Tex. Gen. Laws 1479, was constitutional. Appellee, the State, challenged the district court's order enjoining the issuance of bonds for school districts after September 1, 1995, unless there was efficient funding for educational facilities by then.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, the court affirmed the constitutionality of the public school finance system enacted in Senate Bill 7, 1993 Tex. Gen. Laws 1479. The court vacated the district court's injunction on the issuance of bonds after September 1, 1995, ruling in favor of appellee, the State, and affirmed dismissal of appellant parents' petition in intervention. The court held that Senate Bill 7 did not fail to provide an efficient system of public education as required by Tex. Const. art. VII, § 1, simply because it did not have a separate facilities component. The court upheld the $ 280,000-per-student cap on a district's taxable property, allowing appellee, the State, to tap the reservoirs of taxable property situated in property-rich districts. The court found that under the law, children who lived in property-poor districts and property-rich districts had substantially equal access to the funds necessary for a general diffusion of knowledge. The court upheld the options for school districts to voluntarily reconfigure, the power given to the Commissioner of Education to involuntarily reconfigure districts, and the distribution of excess county education district funds.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the constitutionality of the public school finance system enacted in Senate Bill 7, in all respects, because it provided for general diffusion of knowledge. The court reversed and vacated the district court's judgment enjoining the issuance of bonds after September 1, 1995, thereby ruling in favor of appellee, the State. The court affirmed dismissal of appellant parents' petition because it did not assert justiciable claims.

 

56 of 1793 DOCUMENTS


The State Bar v. Gomez


No. D-4218


SUPREME COURT OF TEXAS


891 S.W.2d 243; 1994 Tex. LEXIS 151; 38 Tex. Sup. J. 140


January 20, 1994, Argued  
December 22, 1994, Delivered


PRIOR HISTORY:   [**1] ON APPLICATION FOR WRIT OF ERROR TO THE COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE THIRD DISTRICT OF TEXAS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, state bar and officers, sought a writ of error to the Court of Appeals For the Third District of Texas, for a determination of whether the trial court had jurisdiction to compel petitioners to provide free legal services to respondent indigent persons. The lower court of appeals' reversed the trial court's dismissal of respondents' suit, which was entered for lack of jurisdiction.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners, state bar and officers, challenged the lower court of appeals' judgment that reversed the dismissal of respondent indigent persons' suit in the trial court. Petitioners sought a writ of error to determine whether the trial court had jurisdiction to hear respondents' suit to compel petitioners to provide services to indigent persons. The court reversed and found that the court possessed the power to regulate the practice of law in the state. The court determined that respondents' case was not justiciable because the dispute between petitioners and respondents would not be resolved by the judicial relief sought. The court's inherent power to regulate the practice of law was not jurisdictional, instead it was administrative supported by the State Bar Act, Tex. Gov't. Code Ann. § 81.011(c). The court noted that the trial court did not have jurisdiction over the dispute and any opinion rendered would have been an advisory opinion prohibited by the Tex. Const. art. II, § 1. The essence of respondents' complaint was that the court failed to establish rules that governed an aspect of attorney conduct, and the trial court had no authority to assume the court's role.


OUTCOME: A judgment that granted jurisdiction to the trial court to hear a suit to compel the provision of services to indigent persons was reversed because the court had the power to regulate the practice of law in the state. The court's power was administrative, the essence of the dispute was that the court did not establish rules that governed an area of attorney conduct, and the trial court lacked authority to assume the court's role.

 

57 of 1793 DOCUMENTS


R.K. v. Ramirez


No. D-4558


SUPREME COURT OF TEXAS


887 S.W.2d 836; 1994 Tex. LEXIS 139; 38 Tex. Sup. J. 52



November 3, 1994, Delivered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Relator doctor was a party in a medical malpractice case brought by a patient who sought discovery of relator's medical and mental health records on the basis that relator's medical and emotional problems affected the care given to the patient. Relator asserted privileges under Tex. R. Civ. Evid. 509 and 510, but the trial court (Texas) ordered production of the records. Relator sought a writ of mandamus from the court.


OVERVIEW: Relator doctor was a party in a medical malpractice action. The patient, the movant in that action, sought discovery of relator's medical and mental health records on the basis that relator's medical and emotional problems affected the patient's treatment. Relator asserted the physician-patient and mental health information privileges under Tex. R. Civ. Evid. 509 and 510. The trial court, however, ordered production of the records and relator sought a writ of mandamus. The court noted that the "offensive use" exception did not apply to the rules in question. Instead, the court established the test for the exceptions under rules 509 and 510 applied only when the records sought were relevant to the condition at issue and the condition was relied upon as part of a party's claim or defense, meaning that the condition was a fact that carries legal significance. The court noted that this test did not violate relator's rights to privacy and granted a conditional writ, ordering the trial court to conduct an in camera inspection of the requested records and to release only those records that met both prongs of the test identified by the court.


OUTCOME: The writ requested by relator doctor was conditionally granted, and the trial court was ordered to conduct an in camera inspection of the patient's requested records and to release only those records which were relevant to a condition at issue in the litigation and the condition itself, relied upon as part of a party's claim or defense, was a fact that carried some legal significance.

Weck v. Sharp


No. 94-0222


SUPREME COURT OF TEXAS


884 S.W.2d 153; 1994 Tex. LEXIS 128; 37 Tex. Sup. J. 1215



September 15, 1994, Delivered


PRIOR HISTORY:   [**1] ON APPLICATION FOR WRIT OF ERROR TO THE THIRD COURT OF APPEALS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner filed an application for writ of error from the Third Court of Appeals (Texas), arguing that he should be able to seek a declaratory judgment that Tex. Tax Code Ann. § 112.051 was unconstitutional and that the Controlled Substances Act was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner purchased approximately 800 pounds of marijuana from undercover officers and was later assessed over one million dollars in tax, penalty, and interest for failure to pay the taxes due for possession of the controlled substance. Petitioner then sued the comptroller and the attorney general seeking a declaratory judgment that Tex. Tax Code Ann. §112.051 was unconstitutional and that the Controlled Substances Act was unconstitutional. The trial court granted the comptroller's plea to the jurisdiction based on petitioner's failure to pay the assessed taxes and the prohibition of declaratory-judgment suits in tax matters. The court reversed and remanded. Noting that it had struck Tex. Tax Code Ann. § 112.108 insofar as it would prevent taxpayers from seeking declaratory judgment on tax cases, the court concluded that the invalidation of Tex. Tax Code Ann. § 112.108 provided the trial court with jurisdiction.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment that granted the comptroller's plea to jurisdiction based on petitioner's failure to pay the assessed taxes and the prohibition of declaratory judgment suits in tax matters. The court concluded that the invalidation of the tax section that prohibited declaratory judgments in tax matters provided the trial court with jurisdiction. Accordingly, the matter was remanded for further proceedings.

Sysco Food Servs. v. Trapnell


No. D-3684


SUPREME COURT OF TEXAS


890 S.W.2d 796; 1994 Tex. LEXIS 112; 37 Tex. Sup. J. 1130; CCH Prod. Liab. Rep. P13,943



June 22, 1994, Delivered


PRIOR HISTORY:   [**1] ON APPLICATION FOR WRIT OF ERROR TO THE COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE THIRTEENTH DISTRICT OF TEXAS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, hash brown, apple pie, and potato whitener manufacturers, appealed the judgment of the Court of Appeals for the Thirteenth District (Texas), which affirmed in part and reversed in part a grant of summary judgment in their favor in respondent relatives' products liability suit alleging negligence, deceptive trade practices, strict liability, and breach of warranty after respondents' relative died from eating petitioners' products.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners, hash brown, apple pie, and potato whitener manufacturers appealed a judgment in respondent relatives' products liability action. The victim died after an allergic reaction to sulfites in foods served on a navy base. Respondent relatives brought suit against petitioners, alleging negligence, deceptive trade practices, strict liability, and breach of warranty and against the Navy, alleging negligence and failure to warn of sulfites. The state court granted summary judgment to respondent food company, and the appellate court reversed. The federal court held that the Navy was not liable. The trial court granted summary judgment to petitioners. The appellate court reversed in part and affirmed in part. On appeal, the court affirmed because a fact issue existed and collateral estoppel could not be applied because doing so would not further the doctrine's goals, despite the fact that the issue of whether potato whitener was in the salad had already been fully litigated in the federal action. The doctrine's purposes were not served because it would not conserve judicial resources, and it was unfair to respondent relatives given the procedural peculiarities of the case.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court's order that petitioners, hash brown, apple pie, and potato whitener manufacturers, were not entitled to summary judgment in respondent relatives' products liability action against them because a fact issue as to causation remained. Further, the court held that the doctrine of collateral estoppel should not be applied because doing so would not serve the doctrine's goals under the circumstances.

 

60 of 1793 DOCUMENTS


C & H Nationwide v. Thompson


NO. D-1326


SUPREME COURT OF TEXAS


903 S.W.2d 315; 1994 Tex. LEXIS 156; 37 Tex. Sup. J. 1059; 42 A.L.R.5th 867



June 22, 1994, DELIVERED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] ON APPLICATION FOR WRIT OF ERROR TO THE COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE FIRST DISTRICT OF TEXAS.

This Opinion Substituted by the Court for Withdrawn Opinion of June 22, 1994, Previously Reported at: 1994 Tex. LEXIS 107.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, driver, truck lessor, and truck company, filed a writ of error regarding the decision of the Court of Appeals of Texas, First District, which affirmed judgment in favor of respondent executrix in a wrongful death action, where petitioners contended, among other things, that the court of appeals erred by not crediting its pretrial payments to respondent against its own liability.


OVERVIEW: Respondent executrix' husband was killed in an automobile accident and appellee brought a wrongful death action against four defendants including petitioners, driver and truck lessor, and company. At trial, the trial court ruled, inter alia, that a settlement by one of the defendants and a partial settlement by petitioners would be credited to respondent's damages before liability was apportioned among the petitioners. Thereafter, the court of appeals affirmed the judgment. On review, the court reversed and remanded the case to the trial court, ruling that petitioners, driver and truck lessor's, pretrial partial payment was properly credited against the whole judgment and not credited back to petitioners. The applicable statute included partial payments towards potential liability as payment toward the whole judgment. The evidence was insufficient to support damages for lost inheritance, because no evidence was presented in the record that showed the value of the estate. Prejudgment interest might be awarded on future damages and appellee was entitled to prejudgment interest on the entire amount of the judgment, including future damages.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded; petitioners' pretrial partial payment was properly credited against the whole damage and not credited back to petitioners, because the applicable statute included partial payments toward potential liability as payment towards the whole damage and that respondent executrix was entitled to prejudgment interest on the entire amount of the judgment.

 

61 of 1793 DOCUMENTS


Cain v. Hearst Corp.


No. D-4171


SUPREME COURT OF TEXAS


878 S.W.2d 577; 1994 Tex. LEXIS 122; 37 Tex. Sup. J. 1151; 22 Media L. Rep. 2161


May 11, 1994, Argued  
June 22, 1994, Delivered


PRIOR HISTORY:   [**1] ON CERTIFIED QUESTION FROM THE UNITED STATES COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE FIFTH CIRCUIT.
Cain v. Hearst Corp., 1 F.3d 345, 1993 U.S. App. LEXIS 21786 (5th Cir. Tex., 1993)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The United States Court of Appeals for the Fifth Circuit certified the questions of whether the state of Texas recognized the tort of false light invasion of privacy, and if so, what statute of limitations governed, after appellant inmate brought an action for the tort against appellee publisher.


OVERVIEW: Appellant inmate filed an action for the tort of false light invasion of privacy against appellee publisher, following appellee's publication of negative references to appellant's character and criminal record. The trial court dismissed the action. The appellate court certified the question of whether Texas recognized the tort of false light invasion of privacy, and if so, what statute of limitations governed the action. The appellate court found that the issues were unsettled under Texas law. The court rejected the false light invasion of privacy tort, citing two reasons. First, the court held that the tort was largely duplicative of other rights of recovery, particularly defamation. The court noted that most of the injuries redressed by the false light tort were also redressed by defamation. Second, the court held that the tort lacked many of the procedural limitations that accompanied actions for defamation, and if the tort were adopted, then the free speech right of citizens under both the Texas and United States Constitutions would be unacceptably derogated. Thus, the court answered the first certified question in the negative, and the second question was rendered moot.


OUTCOME: The court held that Texas did not recognize the tort of false light of invasion of privacy because the tort merely duplicated other, more well-established and procedurally limited torts, such as defamation, and that the tort would unnecessarily abridge free speech under both the Texas and United States Constitutions. In view of the court's holding, the issue of the statute of limitations for the tort was not addressed.

Bridgestone/Firestone, Inc. v. Glyn-Jones


No. D-4097


SUPREME COURT OF TEXAS

Texas Educ. Agency v. Leeper


No. D-2022


SUPREME COURT OF TEXAS


893 S.W.2d 432; 1994 Tex. LEXIS 100; 37 Tex. Sup. J. 968



June 15, 1994, Delivered  
June 15, 1994, Issued


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Concurring and Dissenting Opinion Withdrawn by the Court March 16, 1995; Substituted Concurring and Dissenting Opinion of March 16, 1995, Reported at: 1995 Tex. LEXIS 27.


PRIOR HISTORY: ON APPLICATIONS FOR WRIT OF ERROR TO THE COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE SECOND DISTRICT OF TEXAS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner state agency sought a writ of error regarding the judgment of the Court of Appeals of Texas, Second District, which upheld the trial court's judgment in favor of respondent parents, where petitioner sought review of the district court's construction of private school exemption pursuant to Tex. Educ. Code Ann. § 21.033(a)(1), the propriety of injunctive relief, and respondents' attorney fees award.


OVERVIEW: After petitioner state agency began prosecuting parents of home schooled children as violators of the compulsory attendance law, under Tex. Educ. Code Ann. § 21.032, respondent parents sought injunctive relief to stop the prosecutions. The trial court issued a declaratory judgment construing § 21.032 to include home schooled children within the statute's exemption, permanently enjoined any further prosecutions, and awarded respondents attorney fees. The court of appeals affirmed, on other grounds, and petitioners sought a writ of error. On hearing, the court affirmed and reversed in part the affirmation of the permanent injunction. The court found that, in view of the record, the trial court's declaration of the meaning of "private school" in § 21.033(a)(1), as it related to home schools, was correct. The award of attorney fees was proper because the award was authorized by statute, which waived governmental immunity where the state was a necessary party to the action. The issuance of a permanent injunction was unnecessary because there was no indication that petitioners would attempt to contravene the court's decision.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed judgment in favor of respondent parents and reversed in part the permanent injunction against petitioner state agency. The court found that the trial court's declaration, which included home schools within the statutory exemption, was correct, that attorney fees were proper pursuant to statute, and that a permanent injunction was not warranted because petitioner showed no indication of violating the decision.

 

64 of 1793 DOCUMENTS


City of Lancaster v. Chambers


No. D-3331


SUPREME COURT OF TEXAS


883 S.W.2d 650; 1994 Tex. LEXIS 101; 37 Tex. Sup. J. 980


October 14, 1993, Argued  
June 15, 1994, Delivered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As Corrected September 9, 1994.


PRIOR HISTORY: ON APPLICATION FOR WRIT OF ERROR TO THE COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE FIFTH DISTRICT OF TEXAS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, city and police officers, sought review of the decision of the Court of Appeals for the Fifth District of Texas, which upheld the trial court's grant of summary judgment for petitioners in respondent parents' civil rights action under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983, but which reversed and remanded grant of summary judgment for petitioners on common law negligence. Respondents sought review of the adverse ruling on the § 1983 claim.


OVERVIEW: Respondent parents sued petitioners, city and police officers, for common law negligence, as well as for civil rights violations under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983, after a high-speed police chase seriously injured respondents' son. The lower court affirmed entry of summary judgment for petitioners on § 1983. The lower court reversed the grant of summary judgment for petitioners on state law immunity, and it remanded the negligence claim. On appeal, the court affirmed the § 1983 ruling but not on the same grounds as the lower court. The court held with respect to appellees' substantive due process claim of excessive force under §1983 that petitioner officers did not violate any rights, as the high-speed chase was not so brutal and offensive as to shock the conscience. The court established a new standard of good faith to be applied on remand to determine whether petitioner officers had official immunity with respect to the negligence claim involving performance of discretionary functions. Immunity would apply if a reasonably prudent officer, acting under similar circumstances, would have found that the need to immediately apprehend the suspect outweighed the risk of harm to the public.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed grant of summary judgment for petitioners, city and police officer, on respondents' civil rights claim, as a high-speed case resulting in injury to respondents' son did not shock the conscience. The court remanded respondents' negligence claim after it established a new test for official immunity under state law.

Transp. Ins. Co. v. Moriel


No. D-1507


SUPREME COURT OF TEXAS


879 S.W.2d 10; 1994 Tex. LEXIS 90; 37 Tex. Sup. J. 883



June 8, 1994, Delivered


PRIOR HISTORY:   [**1] ON APPLICATION FOR WRIT OF ERROR TO THE COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE EIGHTH DISTRICT OF TEXAS.

This Opinion Substituted on Overrule of Rehearing for Withdrawn Opinion of February 2, 1994, Previously Reported at: 1994 Tex. LEXIS 23.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner workers' compensation carrier filed an application for writ of error, seeking clarification on the gross negligence standard and the imposition of punitive damages in the context of bad faith insurance litigation. The Court of Appeals for the Eighth District of Texas had affirmed the jury's award of actual damages, mental anguish damages, and punitive damages to respondent injured employee for petitioner's bad faith acts.


OVERVIEW: Under the workers' compensation laws, respondent injured employee's medical costs were to paid by petitioner workers' compensation carrier. Petitioner refused to pay for respondent's medical treatments because the treatment partially involved psychiatric care, even though petitioner had authorized the testing. The trial court awarded punitive damages to respondent for petitioner's bad faith and the appellate court affirmed. After reviewing the record, the court concluded that petitioner's delay or refusal to pay respondent's claim was not gross negligence, it was simply bad faith. The court held that an insurer was liable for punitive damages only if the bad faith tort was accompanied by gross negligence. The existence of mental anguish damages alone did not warrant legal punishment by punitive damages. Here, the evidence did not support a finding that petitioner was actually aware of an extreme risk or a likelihood of serious harm to respondent that was independent and qualitatively different from the inconvenience of an unreasonable delay in payment. The court also instituted procedural standards for punitive damage awards to include bifurcated trials and close scrutiny.


OUTCOME: The court withdrew its earlier opinion on petitioner workers' compensation carrier's motion for rehearing. The court reversed the judgment of the court of appeals, which had affirmed the jury's award of actual, mental anguish, and punitive damages to respondent injured employee. Respondent did not present legally sufficient evidence of gross negligence and was not entitled to punitive damages.

HL Farm Corp. v. Self


No. D-1794


SUPREME COURT OF TEXAS


877 S.W.2d 288; 1994 Tex. LEXIS 74; 37 Tex. Sup. J. 776



May 11, 1994, Delivered


PRIOR HISTORY:   [**1] ON APPLICATION FOR WRIT OF ERROR TO THE COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE FIFTH DISTRICT OF TEXAS.

This Opinion Substituted on Grant of Rehearing for Withdrawn Opinion of January 5, 1994, Previously Reported at: 1994 Tex. LEXIS 11.



DISPOSITION: We reverse the judgment of the court of appeals and remand the cause to the trial court for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: This opinion was substituted for the court's withdrawn opinion of January 5, 1994, regarding petitioner farm corporation's action against respondent, the Kaufman County Single Appraisal District, in which petitioner alleged that Tex. Tax Code Ann. § 23.56(3) violated Tex. Const. art. I, § 3.


OVERVIEW: The court held that Tex. Tax Code Ann. § 23.56(3) violated Tex. Const. art. I, § 3 because no rational basis existed for denying an open-space land designation to a "foreign corporation" owned by a nonresident alien. The court concluded that the classification drawn by Tex. Tax Code Ann. § 23.56(3) violated equal protection, as it was not rationally related to the promotion and preservation of open-space land. Petitioner "foreign corporation," a Virginia corporation whose majority interest was owned by a nonresident alien, a Switzerland corporation, had sought review of the denial of its application for open-space land valuation by respondent, the Kaufman County Single Appraisal District. The trial court granted summary judgment for respondent. The court of appeals affirmed. In this substituted opinion, the court reversed the court of appeals' decision and remanded to the trial court.


OUTCOME: In this substituted opinion, the court held that a state statute violated the Texas Constitution. Accordingly, the court reversed the judgment of the court of appeals in this tax case pertaining to open-space land and remanded the cause to the trial court for further proceedings.

R Communications v. Sharp


No. D-3058


SUPREME COURT OF TEXAS


875 S.W.2d 314; 1994 Tex. LEXIS 97; 37 Tex. Sup. J. 727


March 30, 1993, Argued  
April 28, 1994, Delivered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As Corrected June 16, 1994.


PRIOR HISTORY: ON APPLICATION FOR WRIT OF ERROR TO THE COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE THIRD DISTRICT OF TEXAS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner brought a writ of error from the Court of Appeals for the Third District of Texas that affirmed the decision of the trial court dismissing petitioner's request for judicial review of an administrative hearing finding petitioner liable for sales taxes, penalties and interest.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner sought relief from an assessment for additional sales taxes that resulted from an audit. After an administrative hearing, the petitioner was found to be liable for $ 127,574.46 in taxes. Petitioner sought judicial review, but the trial court dismissed for want of jurisdiction because petitioner had not paid the tax or posted a statutorily required bond under Tex. Tax. Code Ann. §§ 112.051, 112.101. The court of appeals affirmed. On review, the court held that conditioning a taxpayer's right to initiate judicial review on the payment of taxes or the posting of a bond equal to twice the alleged tax obligation violated the open courts mandate of Tex. Const. Art. I, § 13. The court held that sections 111.022 and 112.101 protected the respondent against delays that might impinge its ability to collect taxes. However, the court held that Tex. Tax Code Ann. § 112.108 was unconstitutional and void because it would preclude a taxpayer from obtaining judicial review of its tax liability by means of a declaratory action.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the court of appeals affirming the trial court's decision to dismiss petitioner's request for review of an administrative hearing. The court held that tax statute at issue violated the state's open court's provision by placing an unreasonable financial burden on the complaining parties.

69 of 1793 DOCUMENTS


In the Interest of J.W.T.


No. D-1742


SUPREME COURT OF TEXAS


872 S.W.2d 189; 1994 Tex. LEXIS 21; 37 Tex. Sup. J. 496



February 2, 1994, Delivered


PRIOR HISTORY:   [**1] ON APPLICATION FOR WRIT OF ERROR TO THE COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE NINTH DISTRICT OF TEXAS.

This Opinion Substituted by Court for Withdrawn Opinion of June 30, 1993, Reported at: 1993 Tex. LEXIS 101.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant mother and father brought an application for writ of error challenging the decision of the Court of Appeals for the Ninth District of Texas that reversed the trial court decision. The appellate court held that Tex. Fam. Code § 11.03(a)(7) violated appellee biological father's constitutional right to due process as guaranteed in Tex. Const. art. I § 19.


OVERVIEW: Pending a divorce with appellant father, appellant mother was living with appellee biological father and became pregnant. Appellee arranged for prenatal care at a clinic and acknowledged his paternity. Appellants reconciled and appellant father assumed responsibility for the child. Appellee tried unsuccessfully to maintain contact with the child. Scientific paternity testing showed a 99.41 percent probability that appellee was the child's biological father. Appellee brought action requesting a judicial declaration of paternity and recognition of his visitation rights. The trial court held that appellee lacked standing under Tex. Fam. Code § 11.03(a)(7) to bring any action relating to the child. The court of appeals reversed and determined that Tex. Fam. Code § 11.03(a)(7) violated appellee's right to due process guaranteed by Tex. Const. art. I § 9. The court affirmed the court of appeals and declared Tex. Fam. Code § 11.03(a)(7) and 12.06(a) unconstitutional to the extent that they denied a putative father's standing to sue with regard to a child with a presumed father.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court decision that appellee biological father had standing to bring an action to establish paternity and remanded to the trial court for further proceedings. The court declared unconstitutional those sections of the Texas Family Code that denied a putative father any standing to sue with regard to a child with a presumed father.

 

70 of 1793 DOCUMENTS


State v. Morales


No. D-2393


SUPREME COURT OF TEXAS


869 S.W.2d 941; 1994 Tex. LEXIS 17; 37 Tex. Sup. J. 390



January 12, 1994, Delivered


PRIOR HISTORY:   [**1] ON APPLICATION FOR WRIT OF ERROR TO THE COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE THIRD DISTRICT OF TEXAS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, the State of Texas, sought a writ of error to review a judgment from the Court of Appeals for the Third District (Texas). The court of appeals affirmed the judgment of the trial court, which declared that the sodomy statute, Tex. Penal Code Ann. § 21.06 (1989), was unconstitutional and enjoined the statute's enforcement.


OVERVIEW: Respondents, who were homosexuals, brought a constitutional challenge to the sodomy statute, Tex. Penal Code Ann. § 21.06, claiming that the statute violated their constitutional right to privacy, the due course of law provision under Tex. Const. art. I, §§ 13, 19, and the equal protection provision under Tex. Const. art. I, §§ 3, 3A. The trial court declared that the statute was unconstitutional and enjoined its enforcement; the court of appeals affirmed. The court reversed and remanded with instructions to dismiss. Intervention by an equity court was not proper unless a statute was unconstitutional and there was a threat of irreparable injury to vested property rights. Where there was no actual or threatened enforcement of a statute, a civil court would not enjoin the enforcement of the statute. Moreover, a civil court did not have jurisdiction to render declarations of the legal rights or relationships arising under a statute. If civil courts accepted jurisdiction over such constitutional challenges to criminal statutes, there was a potential for conflicting decisions between the civil and criminal courts of last resort concerning the validity of such statutes.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the declaratory judgment that enjoined the enforcement of the sodomy statute, and the court remanded with instructions to dismiss. A civil court did not have jurisdiction either to enjoin enforcement of a criminal statute where there was no actual or threatened enforcement of the statute or to issue declarations about legal rights arising under the statute.

 

71 of 1793 DOCUMENTS


In re R.A.G.


No. D-4031


SUPREME COURT OF TEXAS


866 S.W.2d 199; 1993 Tex. LEXIS 142; 37 Tex. Sup. J. 205



November 24, 1993, Delivered


PRIOR HISTORY:   [**1] ON APPLICATION FOR WRIT OF ERROR TO THE COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE FIFTH DISTRICT OF TEXAS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner juvenile filed an application for a writ of error, challenging the decision of a Texas Court of Appeals, which affirmed the trial court's order waiving exclusive jurisdiction of petitioner and ordering the case transferred to a criminal district court so that petitioner could be tried as an adult.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner juvenile was accused of capital murder, solicitation of capital murder, and attempted capital murder. The juvenile court waived exclusive jurisdiction and ordered the case transferred to a criminal district court. The court of appeals affirmed. Petitioner brought an application for writ of error, contending that the juvenile court erred in waiving jurisdiction without finding probable cause to believe that he had committed each offense charged. The court granted petitioner's application for writ of error, reversed the judgment of the court of appeals, and vacated the order of the juvenile court. The court found that the juvenile court failed to find that probable cause existed to believe that petitioner committed each offense. The court held that before a juvenile court waives its jurisdiction and order a case transferred to criminal court, the juvenile court had to first find that there was probable cause to believe that the juvenile committed each of the offenses that he was charged with. The court held that if the juvenile was charged with multiple offenses, the juvenile court must expressly dispose of each.


OUTCOME: The court granted petitioner juvenile's application for writ of error, reversed the judgment of the court of appeals, vacated the order of the juvenile court waiving exclusive jurisdiction of petitioner, and remanded the case to the juvenile court for further proceedings. The court held that the juvenile court failed to find that probable cause existed to believe petitioner committed each offense for which he was charged.

Richards v. League of United Latin Am. Citizens


No. D-2197


SUPREME COURT OF TEXAS


868 S.W.2d 306; 1993 Tex. LEXIS 120; 37 Tex. Sup. J. 8



October 6, 1993, Delivered


PRIOR HISTORY:   [**1] ON DIRECT APPEAL FROM THE 107TH JUDICIAL DISTRICT COURT OF CAMERON COUNTY, TEXAS.



DISPOSITION: For the foregoing reasons, we reverse the judgment of the trial court and render judgment in favor of defendants.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, governor and education officials, sought review of the judgment of the 107th Judicial District Court of Cameron County (Texas), in favor of plaintiffs, Mexican American organizations and individuals, in plaintiffs' declaratory judgment action to declare Texas' higher education system unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, Mexican American organizations and individuals, filed a declaratory judgment action against defendants, governor and education officials, and contended that defendants' policies and practices denied Mexican Americans living in Texas' border area participation in higher education programs and access to resources. The trial court found for plaintiffs. Defendants appealed, and the court reversed. The court noted that if the classification complained of was based on geography, then there was not an equal protection violation because defendants had satisfied the rational basis test. The court held that plaintiffs had failed to establish that defendants' system imposed unequal burdens on plaintiffs because there was no direct evidence of defendants' intent to discriminate. Further, there was no showing of a sufficiently high level of disparate impact to raise an inference of intent, and impact evidence did not rise to an adequate level to show discrimination. The court also held that higher education was not a fundamental right, entitled to strict scrutiny review, under the Texas constitution because Tex. Const. art. VII, § 1 applied only to lower levels of education.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment in favor in plaintiffs, Mexican American organizations and individuals, in plaintiffs' action challenging Texas' higher education system because the trial court erred in declaring that the system was unconstitutional because there was no showing of discriminatory intent or impact evidence evincing discrimination, and higher education was not a fundamental right entitled to strict scrutiny review.

The court stayed the injunctive relief granted by the trial court pending further order of the court.

Ex parte Tucci


No. D-2809, No. D-2819, No. D-2820, No. D-2821, No. D-2822, No. D-2823, No. D-2824


SUPREME COURT OF TEXAS


859 S.W.2d 1; 1993 Tex. LEXIS 100; 36 Tex. Sup. J. 1154



June 30, 1993, Delivered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Concurring Opinion Substituted July 14, 1993.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Relator contemnors were held in civil contempt and jailed for disregarding restraining orders that limited demonstrations near abortion clinics during a national convention. Relators brought an original habeas corpus proceeding asserting that the restraining orders were unconstitutional restraints of protected expression under Tex. Const. art. I, § 8. The court released contemnors on bond after they were denied relief by the court of appeals.


OVERVIEW: During a national political convention, relators planned protests at local abortion clinics. The clinics obtained temporary restraining orders to limit the demonstrations so as to protect public access. In disregard of the restraining orders, relators demonstrated within 100 feet of clinic access points, and were held in contempt and committed to county jail. Relators sought habeas relief, asserting that the restraining orders violated Tex. Const. art. I § 8 and were unenforceable. Conceding that the threat of injury to the clinics was both imminent and irreparable, the court noted that the 100-foot zone around each of the clinics encompassed not just clinic property, but public streets and sidewalks as well. In a plurality opinion, the court held that the evidence was insufficient to prove that the 100-foot zone was the least restrictive means for guarding against harassment and intimidation of persons exercising their constitutional right to privacy, or that the unchallenged provisions of the temporary restraining orders provided inadequate protection. The court held that the 100-foot limitation violated Tex. Const. art. I, § 8 and was hence unenforceable against relators.


OUTCOME: The court ordered that relator contemnors, released on bond pending the court's decision, would remain discharged. Without specific findings supported by evidence that the 100-foot zone designated in the temporary restraining orders was the least restrictive means to ensure unimpeded access to abortion clinics and guard against intimidation and harassment, the court held that the limitation unconstitutionally restricted free expression.

In the Interest of J.W.T.


No. D-1742


SUPREME COURT OF TEXAS


872 S.W.2d 189; 1993 Tex. LEXIS 101; 36 Tex. Sup. J. 1126



June 30, 1993, Delivered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [*1] Dissenting Opinion Dated September 15, 1993. Withdrawn February 2, 1994. Substituted Opinion of February 2, 1994, Reported at: 1994 Tex. LEXIS 21. Dissenting Opinion Withdrawn March 30, 1994. Substituted Dissent of March 30, 1994, Reported at: 1994 Tex. LEXIS 46.


PRIOR HISTORY: ON APPLICATION FOR WRIT OF ERROR TO THE COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE NINTH DISTRICT OF TEXAS.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the court of appeals is affirmed and this cause is remanded to the trial court for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant mother and presumed father sought review of a judgment of the Court of Appeals for the Ninth District of Texas, which reversed the dismissal of a paternity action brought by appellee father seeking a declaration of paternity and visitation rights. The court of appeals held that Tex. Fam. Code Ann. § 11.03(a)(7), the legal basis for the trial court's dismissal, violated the due course of law guarantee in Tex. Const. art. I, § 19.


OVERVIEW: Appellee father was the natural father of a child who was conceived and born while appellant mother was married to appellant presumed father. Appellant accepted responsibility as the father of the child by contracting and paying for prenatal care during the mother's pregnancy. After appellants reconciled and prevented appellee from any contact with his child, he filed an action seeking a judicial declaration of paternity and recognition of visitation rights, while acknowledging his responsibility for child support. The trial court dismissed in reliance on Tex. Fam. Code Ann. § 11.03(a)(7), which granted standing only to biological fathers of children who have no presumed father. After thoroughly reviewing the history of laws involving illegitimacy, the court affirmed the holding of the court of appeals that statutes denying standing to a putative father who accepts early parental responsibilities for the child violate the due course of law guarantee of Tex. Const. art. I, § 19. The court noted that its decision was grounded solely upon the Texas due course of law guarantee, which has independent vitality, separate and distinct from the Due Process Clause of U.S. Const. amend. XIV.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the court of appeals that the statute violated the due course of law protections in the Texas Constitution. Therefore, it could not deny appellee father's right to a judicial declaration of paternity. The judgment of the court of appeals was affirmed and the cause was remanded to the trial court for further proceedings.

State v. Flag-Redfern Oil Co.


NO. D-0872, NO. D-0874


SUPREME COURT OF TEXAS


852 S.W.2d 480; 1993 Tex. LEXIS 61; 36 Tex. Sup. J. 929; 118 Oil & Gas Rep. 107

This Opinion Substituted by Court for Withdrawn Opinion of March 3, 1993, Reported at: 1993 Tex. LEXIS 24.



DISPOSITION: For the reasons stated, we affirm the judgments of the court of appeals.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, State of Texas and General Land Office, sought review of the judgment of the court of appeals (Texas) in a dispute involving the authority of petitioner General Land Office to determine contract rights under state mineral leases and to assess deficiencies against respondent oil companies.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners, State of Texas and General Land Office, sought review of the judgment of the court of appeals in a suit challenging the authority of petitioner Texas General Land Office to determine contract rights under state mineral leases. Respondent oil companies were facing deficiency assessments resulting from audits conducted by petitioner land office. Petitioners contended that the trial court's interpretation of Tex. Nat. Res. Code Ann. § 52.135 was inconsistent with the legislature's intent in enacting the 1986 amendments. The court concluded that the intent of the statute was not for the examination and hearing to focus solely on accounting type errors. The court declined to construe the act in manner that would thwart the legislature 's purpose in enacting the statute. The court held that the statute did not offend Tex. Const. art. II §1. The court concluded that the prepayment requirement of the challenged statute offended the open courts provision and constituted an unreasonable interference with access to the courts. The court affirmed the judgment of the court of appeals.


OUTCOME: The court of appeals' finding that petitioner General Land Office did not have the authority to determine contract rights under state mineral leases because the legislature did not intend that examination and hearing focus solely on accounting errors contained in deficiency assessments. Moreover, prepayment requirement of the statute violated the constitutional right of access to the courts and the open courts provision.

Twyman v. Twyman


NO. D-0184


SUPREME COURT OF TEXAS


855 S.W.2d 619; 1993 Tex. LEXIS 59; 36 Tex. Sup. J. 827; 61 U.S.L.W. 2748



May 5, 1993, Delivered


PRIOR HISTORY:   [**1] ON APPLICATION FOR WRIT OF ERROR TO THE COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE THIRD DISTRICT OF TEXAS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner husband filed an application for a writ of error from a judgment of the Court of Appeals for the Third District of Texas, which affirmed an award of negligent infliction of emotional distress to respondent wife in a divorce proceeding.


OVERVIEW: After the husband filed for divorce, the trial court dissolved the parties' marriage, divided the estate, awarded conservatorship of the children, ordered child support, and awarded the wife damages for emotional distress caused by her husband's deviate sexual acts. The husband appealed, and the court of appeals affirmed the judgment. He then applied for a writ of error. The court reversed the judgment, holding that Texas did not recognize the tort of negligent infliction of emotional distress. However, the court adopted the tort of intentional infliction of emotional distress. Consequently, the court remanded the case for a new trial on intentional infliction of emotional distress because the case had proceeded on the theory of negligence. The court further held that there was no legal impediment to bringing a tort claim in a divorce action based on either negligence or an intentional act. Additionally, the court found that the trial court made a disproportionate property division based on the husband's cruel treatment and adultery and that it appeared that such an award allowed the wife a double recovery based on the same conduct.


OUTCOME: The court granted the writ of error and reversed the judgment affirming the award to respondent wife for negligent infliction of emotional distress in a divorce proceeding with petitioner husband because Texas did not recognize the claim of negligent infliction of emotional distress. The court remanded the case for new trial because it adopted the tort of intentional infliction of emotional harm.

Texas Ass'n of Business v. Texas Air Control Bd.


NO. C-9556


SUPREME COURT OF TEXAS


852 S.W.2d 440; 1993 Tex. LEXIS 22; 36 Tex. Sup. J. 607



March 3, 1993, Delivered


PRIOR HISTORY:   [**1] ON DIRECT APPEAL FROM THE 250TH DISTRICT COURT, TRAVIS COUNTY, TEXAS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the decision of the 250th District Court, Travis County (Texas), that upheld the Clean Air Act of Texas, 1967 Tex. Gen. Laws 1941, the Solid Waste Disposal Act, 1969 Tex. Gen. Laws 1320, and the Water Quality Act - Revision, 1969 Tex. Gen. Laws 2229, as constitutional.


OVERVIEW: Appellant brought a declaratory judgment action seeking a ruling that statutes empowering two state administrative agencies to levy civil penalties for violations of their regulations conflicted with the open courts and jury trial provisions of the Texas Constitution. The trial court found the statutes constitutional. On appeal, the court held that the statutes at issue failed to comport with the open courts provision of Tex. Const. art. I, § 13. The court held that citizens must have access to courts unimpeded by unreasonable financial barriers. The court found that, under the statutes at issue, the offender must either tender a cash deposit or post a supersedeas bond in the full amount of the penalties assessed, or forfeit the right to judicial review. The court held that this forfeiture provision was an unreasonable restriction on access to the courts. However, the court found that the regulations did not violate the jury trial provisions of the Tex. Const. art. I, § 15. The court held that certain judicial functions could be delegated constitutionally by the legislature to administrative agencies in furtherance of the preservation of the state's natural resources.


OUTCOME: The court reversed that portion of the trial court judgment that declared the Texas Clean Air Act, Water Code, and Solid Waste Disposal Act did not violate the open courts provision of the Texas Constitution, and affirmed that portion declaring that the listed statutes did not violate the jury trial provision. The court held that the requirement of a supersedeas bond or cash deposit as a prerequisite to judicial review was unconstitutional.

State v. Flag-Redfern Oil Co.


NO. D-0872, NO. D-0874


SUPREME COURT OF TEXAS


1993 Tex. LEXIS 24; 36 Tex. Sup. J. 646



March 3, 1993, Delivered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [*1] Withdrawn May 19, 1993. Substituted Opinion Reported at: 1993 Tex. LEXIS 61.


PRIOR HISTORY: On Application for Writ of Error to the Court of Appeals for the Third District of Texas.



DISPOSITION: For the reasons stated, we affirm the judgments of the court of appeals.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Two cases that involved that authority of petitioner, Texas General Land Office, to determine contract rights under state mineral lease issues came before the court on application for Writ of Error. In each case, the Texas Court of Appeals affirmed a summary judgment that granted declaratory relief to respondent lessees on the ground that the Texas Natural Resource Code did not authorize the petitioner to adjudicate such contract rights.


OVERVIEW: The court affirmed, on grounds other than those articulated by the courts below, summary judgments that granted declaratory relief to respondent lessees in actions to determine contract rights under state mineral leases. The court held that Tex. Nat. Res. Code Ann. § 52.135 authorized the Commissioner of the General Land Office to conduct full-scale audits, encompassing legal as well as factual determinations, and to hold hearings for the redetermination of deficiency assessments. The court held, however, that respondent lessees could not be bound by the determinations made in such hearings. The court stated that a respondent could challenge those determinations in the manner prescribed in Tex. Nat. Res. Code Ann. § 52.137; but that because the prepayment requirement of Tex. Nat. Res. Code Ann. § 52.137(a) offended the open courts provision, Tex. Const. art. I, § 13, respondents did not need to make a protest payment before bringing suit.


OUTCOME: The judgments in favor of respondent lessees were affirmed on grounds other than those articulated below, because though the legislature intended petitioner, Texas General Land Office, to interpret and apply contractual provisions in state mineral leases, the relevant code provisions, insofar as they required respondents to prepay disputed amounts before contesting the agency's determinations in court, were unconstitutional.
