General Chem. Corp. v. De La Lastra


NO. D-1799


SUPREME COURT OF TEXAS


852 S.W.2d 916; 1993 Tex. LEXIS 16; 36 Tex. Sup. J. 574; CCH Prod. Liab. Rep. P13,415



February 24, 1993, Delivered


PRIOR HISTORY:   [**1] ON APPLICATION FOR WRIT OF ERROR TO THE COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE THIRTEENTH DISTRICT OF TEXAS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner chemical corporation sought review of the decision of the Court of Appeals for the Thirteenth District of Texas, which affirmed the trial court's judgment awarding respondents, parents of the deceased, actual damages in their individual capacity, and actual and punitive damages in their capacity as representatives of the decedents' estates in an action brought against petitioner for the wrongful deaths of the deceased.


OVERVIEW: Respondents, the parents of the deceased, filed product's liability and wrongful death actions, in their individual capacities as parents and representatives of the decedents' estates, against petitioner chemical manufacturer after their two sons died from using petitioner's chemical while working off the coast of Texas. The trial court entered a judgment in favor of respondents, based upon state law, and awarded them actual damages, individually, and actual and punitive damages in their survival actions as representatives of the estates. The court of appeals affirmed the decision and petitioner sought review. The court affirmed the appellate court's determination that state law applied because, although petitioner properly invoked maritime law, it waived this issue by failing to object to evidence and jury questions regarding damages that were not recoverable under maritime law. The court reversed the appellate court's award of punitive damages because it exceeded the amount permitted under Tex. Const. art. XVI, § 26 and Tex. Civ. Prac. & Rem. Code § 41.007, which limited punitive damages to four times the actual damages awarded to respondents in the survival causes of action.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court's judgment that state law, and not maritime law, applied in the case, but reversed the appellate court's award of punitive damages, because they exceeded the maximum amount permitted by the constitution and statute, and remanded the case to the trial court with instructions to recalculate punitive damages, limiting them to four times the actual damages awarded in the survival actions.

Speer v. Presbyterian Children's Home


NO. D-2170


SUPREME COURT OF TEXAS


847 S.W.2d 227; 1993 Tex. LEXIS 13; 36 Tex. Sup. J. 511



February 3, 1993, Delivered


PRIOR HISTORY:   [**1] ON APPLICATION FOR WRIT OF ERROR TO THE COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE FIFTH DISTRICT OF TEXAS.



DISPOSITION: Accordingly, without reference to the merits, we vacate the judgment of the court of appeals and of the trial court, and dismiss this case as moot.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, applicant and Texas Commission on Human Rights, sought a writ of error to the Court of Appeals for the Fifth District (Texas) which held that respondent agency's action fell under the statutory exemption for religious organizations, Tex Rev. Civ. Stat. Ann. art. 5221k, §5.06(1) (1987), in a case regarding unlawful discrimination based on religious affiliation. Respondent contended that the case was moot and should be dismissed.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner applicant sought a position with respondent agency. Respondent was a nonprofit Texas corporation founded by and affiliated with the Presbyterian Church. Petitioner applicant was sent a rejection letter informing her that respondent hired only Christians; petitioner applicant was Jewish. Petitioner applicant filed a claim for unlawful discrimination with petitioner Texas Commission on Human Rights. Both petitioners filed causes of action against respondent. At trial, petitioner applicant sought an injunction compelling respondent to hire the most qualified person for that particular position and declaratory relief. The court found that declaratory relief was improper because the action sought to be enjoined was accomplished. Suitable coercive relief became impossible because respondent withdrew from offering adoption services, and the position petitioner applicant wanted was abolished. Accordingly, the court, without reference to the merits, vacated the judgment of the lower court and dismissed the case as moot.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the lower court's judgment finding that respondent's actions fell under statutory exemption for religious organizations because the cause of action was moot when the declaratory relief sought was unavailable.

 

82 of 1793 DOCUMENTS


Guillory v. Port of Houston Auth.


NO. D-1391


SUPREME COURT OF TEXAS


845 S.W.2d 812; 1993 Tex. LEXIS 1; 36 Tex. Sup. J. 474



January 20, 1993, Delivered


PRIOR HISTORY:   [**1] ON APPLICATION FOR WRIT OF ERROR TO THE COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE FIRST DISTRICT OF TEXAS.



DISPOSITION: Finding no error in the court of appeals' decision, we affirm the judgment of that court.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner longshoreman sought a writ of error to the Court of Appeals for the First District of Texas which limited the liability of respondent port authority under the Texas Tort Claims Act, Tex. Civ. Prac. & Rem. Code §§101.001-.109.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner longshoreman was unloading cargo when the truck he was driving flipped backwards and landed in the ship's hold. The truck was leased from respondent port authority, which owned it. Respondent was a navigation district created under Tex. Const. art. XVI, §59 (1927). Petitioner recovered $ 956,781.91 in damages from respondent, and respondent appealed. The lower court reformed the judgment to limit respondent's liability to $ 100,000 as required by the Texas Tort Claim act, Tex. Civ. Prac. & Rem. Code §§101.001(2)(B), 101.025, 101.051. Petitioner argued that a limitation on immunity should occur when the governmental entity was involved in activities that a private entity could do. The court held that to waive immunity in this context would virtually vitiate immunity entirely because there were only a few activities that were uniquely governmental. The court found that applying immunity based upon the creation of the governmental unit instead of its function resulted in certain anomalies and inconsistencies. However, the lower court's judgment was affirmed and no error was found because any waiver of immunity needed to occur in the Texas legislature.


OUTCOME: The court found no error in the lower court's decision to limit petitioner's recovery and affirmed the judgment of the lower court because respondent was protected by governmental immunity under Texas law.

Dallas Morning News, Inc. v. Fifth Court of Appeals


NO. D-2991


SUPREME COURT OF TEXAS


842 S.W.2d 655; 1992 Tex. LEXIS 137; 36 Tex. Sup. J. 100



October 21, 1992, Delivered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As Corrected October 23, 1992. Concurring Opinion by Phillips Withdrawn November 11, 1992. Substituted Concurring Opinion Reported at 1992 Tex. LEXIS 147. Dissenting Opinion by Doggett Withdrawn November 11, 1992. Substituted Dissenting Opinion Reported at 1992 Tex. LEXIS 147.


PRIOR HISTORY: ON PETITION FOR WRIT OF MANDAMUS TO THE COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE FIFTH DISTRICT OF TEXAS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Relator, the media and public citizens, filed an application for writ of mandamus with the supreme court and requested that the Court of Appeals for the Fifth District of Texas be ordered to withdraw its order limiting access to certain trial court exhibits.


OVERVIEW: Individuals brought an action against a pharmaceutical company and claimed that a drug produced by that company caused them to harm themselves and others. Months before trial, the drug company sought protection from certain documents already provided during discovery by filing a motion under Tex. R. Civ. P. 166b for a protective order or, alternatively, a Tex. R. Civ. P. 76a sealing order. The trial court denied those motions and, when trial commenced, the appeal of that trial court ruling was pending in the court of appeals, which issued a temporary order limiting disclosure to those involved with trial until further order from the court of appeals. Relator, the media and public citizens, then sought a writ of mandamus to force the court of appeals to withdraw its order limiting access to trial exhibits. The supreme court denied relators' application for writ of mandamus and held that the court of appeals was authorized to issue its order protecting jurisdiction over an appeal pursuant to Tex. R. Civ. P. 76a. The court found no law that gave the press or public unlimited access to trial exhibits, as relators had requested.


OUTCOME: The supreme court denied the application for writ of mandamus filed by relator, the media and public citizens, and held that the court of appeals was authorized to enter its order that limited disclosure of trial court exhibits and protect its jurisdiction over an appeal.

 

84 of 1793 DOCUMENTS


Russell v. Ingersoll-Rand Co.


NO. D-0489


SUPREME COURT OF TEXAS


841 S.W.2d 343; 1992 Tex. LEXIS 109; 36 Tex. Sup. J. 58; CCH Prod. Liab. Rep. P13,345



October 14, 1992, Delivered


PRIOR HISTORY:   [**1] ON APPLICATION FOR WRIT OF ERROR TO THE COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE FIRST DISTRICT OF TEXAS.



DISPOSITION: We therefore affirm the judgment of the court of appeals.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, decedent's estate administrator and heirs, challenged the judgment of the Court of Appeals for the First District of Texas, which affirmed the judgment of the district court dismissing appellants' wrongful death and survivorship action against appellee manufacturers based on the statute of limitations.


OVERVIEW: Decedent brought an action against certain manufacturers after he developed pulmonary disease as a result of his exposure to silica. He died during the pendency of his action, and appellants, decedent's estate administrator and heirs, named themselves as plaintiffs and added appellee manufacturers as defendants. Appellees brought motions for summary judgment based on the statute of limitations. The trial court granted summary judgment, which was affirmed by the court of appeals. Appellants submitted an application for writ of error to the court, alleging that the statute of limitations applicable to survivorship and wrongful death actions set forth in Tex. Civ. Prac. & Rem. Code Ann. § 16.003(b), began to run upon decedent's death. The court affirmed the judgments and held that if decedent would have been barred by the statute of limitations from bringing the cause of action immediately prior to his death, then any wrongful death or survivorship action based on the same set of facts was also be barred.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgments dismissing the wrongful death and survivor claims brought by appellants, decedent's estate administrator and heirs, against appellant manufacturers based on the statute of limitations. Because the statute of limitations would have barred such an action if he had brought by it immediately prior to his death, then survivorship and wrongful death actions based on the same occurrence were also barred.

 

85 of 1793 DOCUMENTS


Religious of Sacred Heart v. Houston


No. D-1320


SUPREME COURT OF TEXAS


836 S.W.2d 606; 1992 Tex. LEXIS 94; 35 Tex. Sup. J. 1066



July 1, 1992, DELIVERED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] ON APPLICATION FOR WRIT OF ERROR TO THE COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE FIRST DISTRICT OF TEXAS


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner condemnee sought review of a judgment from the Court of Appeals for the First District of Texas, which held that the substitute facilities doctrine did not apply to the partial taking by respondent condemnor of a private school.


OVERVIEW: A condemnation action was submitted to the jury on the theory that petitioner condemnee was entitled to compensation from respondent condemnor for the cost of purchasing substitute facilities and the lower court reversed the award. The court affirmed the judgment of the lower court and held that the substitute facilities doctrine did not apply to the taking of a private school. The court found that a departure from the market value concept was not justified even though petitioner's situation presented unique and sympathetic facts. The court held that respondent's objection to the charge on the ground that the substitute facilities doctrine did not apply to private schools was sufficient to preserve error regarding the erroneous submission of the case to the jury on the substitute facilities doctrine. The court also held that respondent did not waive the market value measure of compensation. Respondent correctly recognized that the most common method of determining market value could not be successfully utilized in this situation, resorted to additional methods, and argued the applicability of these methods and offered evidence regarding their application.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment against petitioner condemnee and remanded the cause to the trial court for further proceedings. The substitute facilities doctrine did not apply to the partial taking of a private school and respondent condemnor did not waive the application of the market value measure of compensation.

Davenport v. Garcia


No. D-1558


SUPREME COURT OF TEXAS


834 S.W.2d 4; 1992 Tex. LEXIS 76; 35 Tex. Sup. J. 894



June 17, 1992, Delivered


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] ON PETITION FOR WRIT OF MANDAMUS TO THE 151ST JUDICIAL DISTRICT COURT OF HARRIS COUNTY, TEXAS


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Relator guardian ad litem sought a writ of mandamus to the 151st Judicial District Court of Harris County, Texas. Relator contended that the court's gag orders violated the guarantee of free expression contained in Tex. Const. art. I, § 8.


OVERVIEW: In a suit concerning toxic chemical exposure at a dump site, the trial judge appointed relator, to be guardian ad litem for 213 children who were also plaintiffs. After 18 months, a hearing was held and the trial judge entered a gag and protective order, instructing the guardian ad litem, parties and counsel to cease and desist any discussion of the case outside the court hearing and prohibiting any communications with any other lawyer or discussion at all about the matters that had transpired in the case. The guardian ad litem was subsequently dismissed by the trial judge. Relator then brought this writ of mandamus action challenging the trial court orders. The court held that the gag orders violated Tex. Const. art. I, § 8. The orders failed to identify any miscommunication that the trial court may have perceived, did not indicate any specific, imminent harm to the litigation, and offered no explanation of why such harm could not be sufficiently cured by remedial action.


OUTCOME: The court granted a partial writ of mandamus finding that the trial court gag order's violated the state's constitutional guarantee of free expression. The court invalidated the trial court's unconstitutional prior restraint on speech. Because the trial court did not otherwise abuse its discretion, the remainder of the petition was denied.

State v. Windham


No. D-0619


SUPREME COURT OF TEXAS


837 S.W.2d 73; 1992 Tex. LEXIS 71; 35 Tex. Sup. J. 829



June 10, 1992, DELIVERED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] ON APPLICATION FOR WRIT OF ERROR TO THE COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE FOURTEENTH DISTRICT OF TEXAS


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner state appealed from a decision of the Court of Appeals for the Fourteenth District (Texas) in a condemnation action involving a partial taking. The court was to determine whether the trial court erred by excluding evidence offered by petitioner state, the condemnor, controverting that offered by respondent landowners as to the appropriate economic unit to be considered by the jury for determining the market value of the part taken.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner state, through its highways department, condemned 2 acres out of a 19-acre tract belonging to respondent landowners to widen an existing highway. A panel of special commissioners determined the award of adequate compensation to be paid by the state. Respondents objected to the award and appealed to the county court. In the county court, respondents filed a motion in limine to exclude evidence of the market value of the 19-acre tract as a whole, contending that use of an averaging method of valuation resulted in unconstitutional compensation. The county court granted the motion in limine. The state was not allowed to present evidence for its position that the market value of the condemned land should be based on an economic unit consisting of the entire tract and the highest and best use of the land, though its expert testified subject to the motion's limitation. The county court rendered a jury verdict affirmed by the appeals court. The state appealed from both judgments. The court reversed the lower courts' judgments, finding reversible error because the lower court jury was not able to consider all competing evidence of valuation, and remanded for a new trial.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appeals court decision that affirmed the trial court decision to exclude evidence offered by the state controverting that offered by respondent landowners, which had been admitted into evidence regarding the appropriate economic unit the jury was to consider. The court, concluding that reversible error occurred, remanded the case for a new trial.

First Baptist Church v. Bexar County Appraisal Review Bd.


No. D-0689


SUPREME COURT OF TEXAS


833 S.W.2d 108; 1992 Tex. LEXIS 72; 35 Tex. Sup. J. 835



June 10, 1992, DELIVERED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] ON APPLICATION FOR WRIT OF ERROR TO THE COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE FOURTH DISTRICT OF TEXAS


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner church sought a writ of error from the Court of Appeals for the Court of Appeals for the Fourth District of Texas. Petitioner contended that the appellate court erred when it reversed the trial court and held that the parking lots owned by petitioner were not tax-exempt.


OVERVIEW: The trial court held that two parking lots owned by petitioner church and leased to a realty company were exempt from property taxes. The appellate court reversed the trial court and held that there was no evidence that the parking lots were primarily used for religious purposes to justify a tax exemption. The court stated whether a church parking lot was tax-exempt depended upon whether the lots were used primarily for religious purposes. The court noted that testimony indicated that the lots were purchased to ensure adequate parking for church members attending services. The court noted that 40 spaces were reversed for the church at all times and the remaining spaces were reserved for church use at all times except weekdays from 7:30 a.m. to 5:00 p.m. The court held that there was evidence to support the jury's finding that the parking lots were used primarily for religious purposes. Because the appellate court failed to address respondent county appraisal review board's other allegations of error, the court remanded the cause to the appellate court for consideration of all unaddressed points.


OUTCOME: Judgment of the appellate court which reversed the trial court and held that there was no evidence that the parking lots were primarily used for religious purposes to justify a tax exemption was reversed because there was some evidence which supported the jury verdict. The cause was remanded for the appellate court to consider all previously unaddressed points of error.

Camacho v. Samaniego


No.D-1425


SUPREME COURT OF TEXAS


831 S.W.2d 804; 1992 Tex. LEXIS 57; 35 Tex. Sup. J. 734



May 6, 1992, DELIVERED


PRIOR HISTORY:   [*1] ON APPLICATION FOR WRIT OF ERROR TO THE COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE EIGHTH JUDICIAL DISTRICT OF TEXAS


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners bail bondsmen sought review of the decision by the Court of Appeals for the Eighth Judicial District of Texas which granted respondents, sheriff and county, summary judgment upholding a fee and declaring that a statutory county court had no constitutional authority to hear their case.


OVERVIEW: Pursuant to an authorization of respondent county's commissioners court, respondent sheriff collected a fee on surety bail bonds filed for the release from the county jail. Petitioners bail bondsmen challenged the fee. Pursuant to an amendment of Tex. Gov't. Code Ann. § 25.0732(b) (1992), the trial court held that it had jurisdiction to review the actions of the commissioners court and granted summary judgment for respondents. The court of appeals reversed, finding that the trial court had no jurisdiction and dismissed the cause. Although the lawsuit was not within the trial court's express jurisdiction, the court reversed the court of appeals and held that the trial court properly exercised jurisdiction under Tex. Gov't. Code Ann. § 25.0732(t) and that the section was constitutional. However, the court rendered a declaration that the fee was illegal. Because no article of the Code of Criminal Procedure provided that a county commissioners court could have levied a fee against petitioners, and because Tex. Local Gov't. Code Ann. § 118.131 did not apply in the criminal context, Tex. Code Crim. Proc. Ann. art. 103.002 forbade respondent sheriff from imposing the disputed fee.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the court of appeals and held that the trial was constitutionally conducted in a court of proper jurisdiction. However, the court also reversed the trial court's summary judgment for respondent sheriff and rendered a declaration of petitioner bail bondsmen that the fee was illegal because it found that the bond approval fees were not authorized by any Texas statute. The court remanded to the trial court.

Delaney v. University of Houston


No. D-0138


SUPREME COURT OF TEXAS


835 S.W.2d 56; 1992 Tex. LEXIS 48; 35 Tex. Sup. J. 685



April 29, 1992, delivered


PRIOR HISTORY:   [**1] ON APPLICATION FOR WRIT OF ERROR TO THE COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE FOURTEENTH DISTRICT OF TEXAS


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner university student filed an application for writ of error to the Court of Appeals for the Fourteenth District of Texas, which affirmed a district court's grant of summary judgment to respondent university on the ground that petitioner's causes of action were barred by governmental immunity because they arose out of an intentional tort. Petitioner had been raped by an armed intruder in her campus dormitory room.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner enrolled at respondent university after it assured her it would provide her a safe place of residence. Petitioner was raped by an armed intruder in her campus dormitory room. Petitioner sued, claiming damages for her injuries from respondent and alleging that respondent breached its contract with her, breached express and implied warranties to her, was negligent, and violated a state deceptive trade practices-consumer protection act. A divided court of appeals affirmed the district court's grant of summary judgment for respondent on the ground that petitioner's causes of action were barred by governmental immunity because they arose out of an intentional tort. On further appeal, the court reversed, finding error in the lower courts' conclusion that petitioner's claims were barred as a matter of law by the Texas Tort Claims Act, Tex. Civ. Prac. & Rem. Code Ann. § 101.057(2). The court found that § 101.057(2) did not specify whether the tortfeasor had to be the governmental employee or could be a third party, but that the more plausible reading of the provision was the tortfeasor had to be the governmental employee whose conduct was the subject of the complaint.


OUTCOME: The court reversed a judgment which affirmed a grant of summary judgment to respondent university in petitioner university student's action for breach of contract, breach of express and implied warranties, negligence, and violation of a state deceptive trade practices act, and the court remanded the case. The court found that petitioner's action was not barred by the Texas Tort Claims Act because the tortfeasor was not a university employee.

Caller-Times Pub. Co. v. Triad Communications, Inc.


No. C-9979


SUPREME COURT OF TEXAS


826 S.W.2d 576; 1992 Tex. LEXIS 20; 35 Tex. Sup. J. 509; 1992-1 Trade Cas. (CCH) P69,746



February 26, 1992, Delivered


PRIOR HISTORY:   [**1] ON APPLICATION FOR WRIT OF ERROR TO THE COURT OF APPEALS FOR THE THIRTEENTH DISTRICT OF TEXAS.

Original Opinion of November 13, 1991, Reported at 1991 Tex. LEXIS 136 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner newspaper publisher appealed the judgment of the Court of Appeals for the Thirteenth District of Texas, which affirmed the trial court's judgment that petitioner violated the Texas Free Enterprise and Antitrust Act of 1983, Tex. Bus. & Com. Code Ann. §§ 15.01-15.51, by engaging in predatory pricing when it called on respondent advertiser's customers and offered them special rates at prices below petitioner's average total cost.


OVERVIEW: Respondent advertiser filed suit against petitioner newspaper publisher, claiming that petitioner violated the Texas Free Enterprise and Antitrust Act of 1983, Tex. Bus. & Com. Code Ann. §§ 15.01-15.51, when it engaged in predatory pricing practices by targeting respondent's customers. The trial court entered judgment in favor of respondent. The court of appeals affirmed, holding that petitioner engaged in predatory pricing by charging below its average total cost. On review, the court reversed in part, holding that the court of appeals used an incorrect measure of cost. The court found that petitioner did not engage in predatory pricing because respondent failed to introduce any evidence that petitioner charged below its average variable cost. The court remanded to the court of appeals for consideration of respondent's tortious interference with business relations claim. The court ruled that upon respondent's request, the court of appeals would remand to the trial court for a new trial on respondent's antitrust claims. The court determined that although respondent could only have judgment rendered on one of alternative theory, respondent could elect the more favorable judgment.


OUTCOME: The court reversed in part the court of appeals' judgment holding that petitioner newspaper publisher violated the Texas Antitrust Act as respondent advertiser failed to introduce evidence that petitioner engaged in predatory pricing, and remanded to have judgment rendered on respondent's tortious interference with business relations claims. Thereafter, respondent could request remand to the trial court for a new trial on its antitrust claims.

Dawkins v. Meyer


No. D-2032


SUPREME COURT OF TEXAS


825 S.W.2d 444; 1992 Tex. LEXIS 22; 35 Tex. Sup. J. 540



February 25, 1992, Delivered


PRIOR HISTORY:   [**1] ORIGINAL PROCEEDING


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Relator state board member applied for candidacy in the Republican primary for state representative and was approved by respondent state chairman. Respondent later withdrew the approval contending that relator held a lucrative office of the state in an overlapping term. Relator filed an application for leave to file a writ of mandamus to the Texas Supreme Court requesting that the court reinstate her certification.


OVERVIEW: Relator state board member was an appointed member of the Board of the Texas Department of Mental Health and Mental Retardation, whose term did not expire until after the next regular term of the Texas Legislature began. Relator applied with respondent state chairman for candidacy as a state representative. Respondent initially approved relator's certification; however, he revoked it because public records showed that relator held, for an overlapping term, a lucrative state office. Relator filed her application for a writ of mandamus with the court contending that her appointed office was not lucrative because its expenses were greater than the compensation paid. The court denied relator's application because it found that any compensation, no matter how meager, rendered the office lucrative and that the amount of salary or compensation attached to an office was not material. The court refused to carve an exception to Tex. Const. art. III § 19 pursuant to the doctrine of ejusdem generis.


OUTCOME: The court denied relator's application for writ of mandamus finding that her overlapping appointed office was lucrative because she received some compensation from it although her expenses for the office far exceeded her compensation.

Carrollton-Farmers Branch Indep. Sch. Dist. v. Edgewood Indep. Sch. Dist.


NO. D-1469, ET AL.1


1 This proceeding consists of five direct appeals from judgments in three district courts. We noted probable jurisdiction over these appeals and, because all of them involve similar contentions, consolidated them for argument and decision. 35 TEX. SUP. CT. J. 10 (October 9, 1991). Three of the five consolidated cases are appeals from the judgment rendered August 27, 1991, and made final after severance in Edgewood Indep. Sch. Dist., et al. v. Meno, No. 362,516-A in the 250th District Court in Travis County, Texas: Cause No. D-1469, Carrollton-Farmers Branch Indep. Sch. Dist., et al. v. Edgewood Indep. Sch. Dist., et al., Cause No. D-1477, Andrews Indep. Sch. Dist., et al. v. Edgewood Indep. Sch. Dist., et al., and Cause No. D-1560, Highland Park Indep. Sch. Dist., et al. v. Edgewood Indep. Sch. Dist., et al.; Cause No. D-1493, McCarty, et al. v. County Education Dist. No. 21. et al., is an appeal from the judgment rendered September 4, 1991, in Cause No. 2962 in the 18th District Court in Somervell County, Texas. Cause No. D-1544, Eliodoro Reyes, et al. v. Mitchell County Education Dist., et al., is an appeal from the judgment rendered September 5, 1991, in Cause No. 12,195 in the 32nd District Court in Mitchell County, Texas.


SUPREME COURT OF TEXAS


826 S.W.2d 489; 1992 Tex. LEXIS 7; 35 Tex. Sup. J. 374



January 30, 1992, Delivered


PRIOR HISTORY:   [**1] ON DIRECT APPEAL


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In this consolidated action, appellants school districts and individual citizens challenged the judgments of three district courts (Texas) in five separate cases, which found the state public school finance system devised by S. 351, 72nd Leg., Reg. Sess. (Tex. 1991) constitutional.


OVERVIEW: Appellants school districts and individual citizens challenged the constitutionality of the state public school finance system devised by S. 351, 72nd Leg., Reg. Sess. (Tex. 1991) (the bill). In five separate actions, three district courts found the bill constitutional, and appellants challenged. The court held that the bill levied a state ad valorem tax in violation of Tex. Const. art. VIII, §1-e because it created county education districts (CEDs) to levy a uniform tax statewide and the distribution of the proceeds was set by the bill. No CED had any discretion in determining either the tax rate or the distribution. The court further held that the bill violated Tex. Const. art. VII, § 3 because it levied an ad valorem tax without an election. Although appellants claimed that the bill violated Tex. Const. art. III, §§ 56 and 64(a), the court found no such violation because it was not a special or local law and no school districts were consolidated. Having concluded that the bill was unconstitutional; the court then applied the Chevron test and held that its decision was prospective in nature. Thus, the CEDs were permitted to collect all 1991 and 1992 taxes under this system.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgments of the district courts and held that the state public school finance system was unconstitutional because it levied a state ad valorem tax and allowed the levy of such taxes without an election. The court, however, applied its decision prospectively and allowed the collection of taxes under this system for prior tax years.

Terrazas v. Ramirez


No. D-1817


SUPREME COURT OF TEXAS


829 S.W.2d 712; 1991 Tex. LEXIS 160; 35 Tex. Sup. J. 256



December 17, 1991, Delivered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Rehearing Denied January 15, 1992, Reported at 1992 Tex. LEXIS 1.
Rehearing denied by Terrazas v. Ramirez, 1992 Tex. LEXIS 1, 35 Tex. Sup. Ct. J. 308 (Tex., 1992)


PRIOR HISTORY: ON APPLICATION FOR WRIT OF MANDAMUS TO THE 332ND DISTRICT COURT IN HIDALGO COUNTY, TEXAS
Richards v. Mena, 820 S.W.2d 371, 1991 Tex. LEXIS 125 (Tex., 1991)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Relators sought a writ of mandamus to respondent judge of the 332nd District Court of Hidalgo County (Texas). Relators sought to direct respondent to vacate two judgments that ordered that all elections for the Texas Senate and House of Representatives be based, not upon reapportionment statutes enacted by the Texas Legislature, but upon districts reconfigured by the district court.


OVERVIEW: In an original proceeding, relators, non-parties in two suits that challenged a reapportionment plan, sought a writ of mandamus vacating the judgments in the suits. The judgments were based on a settlement agreement between plaintiffs in both cases and top state officials. In litigation challenging the accuracy of the 1990 census and the validity of any redistricting based upon it, respondent judge ordered reconfigured senatorial districts based, not upon the reapportionment statute enacted by the state legislature, but upon districts reconfigured by respondent judge. Relators argued that respondent judge, in redistricting the state by merely approving an agreement of some state officials and a few individuals, had encroached upon the legislative power. The court ruled that respondent judge abused his discretion by ordering a reapportionment without a determination that the legislative reapportionment plan was invalid. The court held that relators were entitled to relief by mandamus because the procedural complexities of both cases denied any other effective relief to relators and because request and refusal was not required for mandamus relief in every case.


OUTCOME: The court held that relators were entitled to mandamus and directed the lower court to vacate its judgments because the lower court abused its discretion in ordering a reapportionment plan without a determination that the legislative reapportionment plan was invalid. The court held that a district court could not order a reapportionment plan based upon nothing more than an agreement between some state officials and a few citizens.

 

95 of 1793 DOCUMENTS


Orange County v. Ware


No. C-9611


SUPREME COURT OF TEXAS


819 S.W.2d 472; 1991 Tex. LEXIS 143; 35 Tex. Sup. J. 150



November 20, 1991, Delivered


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] On Direct Appeal From the 260th District Court of Orange County, Texas.

Original Opinion of December 31, 1990, Reported at 1990 Tex. LEXIS 155.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant county challenged a decision of the 260th District Court of Orange County (Texas) that issued a temporary injunction against appellant to prevent appellant from garnishing appellee commissioner's wages to satisfy appellee's bail bond forfeiture debts. The trial court held that appellant's actions in compliance with Tex. Loc. Gov't Code Ann. § 154.025 were in direct conflict with Tex. Const. art. XVI, § 28. Appellee sought a rehearing.


OVERVIEW: Appellant county sought review of the issuance of a temporary injunction by the trial court that prevented appellant from withholding payment of appellee commissioner's compensation. The trial court held that appellant's action under Tex. Loc. Gov't Code Ann. § 154.025 was in direct conflict with Tex. Const. art. XVI, § 28. Appellee sought a rehearing. The court denied appellee's motion for a rehearing, reversed the trial court decision, and remanded. The court held that there was no legal basis for the temporary injunction. Appellant's withholding of appellee's compensation until satisfaction of appellee's bail bond forfeiture debts was proper. Appellant's threatened withholding of appellee's salary involved only appellee and appellant and not a third party as necessary for a constitutionally prohibited garnishment. The offset of mutual obligations between the parties was not garnishment.


OUTCOME: The court denied appellee commissioner's rehearing motion, reversed a temporary injunction issued by the trial court, and remanded. Appellant county was not constitutionally prohibited from withholding appellee's salary until appellee satisfied bail bond forfeiture debts. The threatened salary withholding was not a prohibited garnishment because the only parties involved were appellee and appellant.

Texas Workers' Compensation Comm'n v. Garcia


No. D-1516


SUPREME COURT OF TEXAS


817 S.W.2d 60; 1991 Tex. LEXIS 114; 35 Tex. Sup. J. 26



October 16, 1991 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] On Direct Appeal from an Order of the 365th District Court of Maverick County, Texas.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, state workers' compensation commission and its director, challenged judgment from an order of the 365th District Court of Maverick County (Texas) declaring the Texas Workers' Compensation Act, Tex. Rev. Civ. Stat. Ann. art. 8308-1.01 et seq. (1991), void as violative of various provisions of the Texas Constitution.


OVERVIEW: Appellees sought injunctive and other relief against appellants, state workers' compensation commission and its director, and against a private employer. At trial, appellees withdrew their injunctive claims. Injunctive relief was not granted as to the private employer. Pursuant to Tex. Gov't. Code Ann. § 22.001(c), an appeal could be taken directly to the supreme court from an order of a trial court granting or denying an interlocutory or permanent injunction on the ground of the constitutionality of a state statute. Appellant's sought review to the supreme court on that basis. Jurisdiction was challenged, and the supreme court held that because declaratory relief and not injunctive relief was granted by the trial court, the supreme court did not have jurisdiction to hear the case. Therefore, the appeal was dismissed for want of jurisdiction.


OUTCOME: Appellants', state workers' compensation commission and its director, challenge to an order declaring a state statute void as violative of the state constitution was dismissed for want of jurisdiction. The trial court entered declaratory judgment, but injunctive relief was not granted; therefore, the state supreme court did not have jurisdiction over the direct appeal.

 

97 of 1793 DOCUMENTS


General Electric Credit Corp. v. Midland Cent. Appraisal Dist.


No. D-1170


SUPREME COURT OF TEXAS


826 S.W.2d 124; 1991 Tex. LEXIS 116; 35 Tex. Sup. J. 25



October 16, 1991, Delivered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: On application for writ of error, defendant taxpayer challenged a judgment from the court of appeals (Texas) that, after affirming a trial court's judgment granting plaintiff taxing authorities summary judgment, awarded damages under Tex. R. App. P. 84 to each plaintiff in an amount 10 times the total taxable costs.


OVERVIEW: Defendant taxpayer sought a writ of error in challenge to a judgment that affirmed a trial court's entry of summary judgment in favor of plaintiff taxing authorities and awarded plaintiffs damages, under Tex. R. App. P. 84 (Rule 84), in the amount of 10 times the total taxable costs. In the underlying suit, defendant challenged plaintiffs' tax assessments against defendant's airplane, arguing that determination of the plane's legal situs was erroneous and that relevant sections of the state tax code were unconstitutional. The court granted defendant a writ of error and reversed that part of the judgment that awarded plaintiffs Rule 84 damages. The court ruled that while defendant failed to exhaust its administrative remedied before filing suit, as mandated by the tax code, defendant had at least an arguable basis for refusing to follow the tax code provisions, including cases holding that a party who availed itself of the benefits of the tax code was barred from challenging constitutionality of that statute. Thus, because defendant's argument had a reasonable basis in law and constituted an informed, good-faith challenge to a trial court judgment, the damages was not justified.


OUTCOME: The court granted defendant taxpayer a writ of error and reversed a judgment that awarded plaintiff taxing authorities damages following defendant's unsuccessful court challenge to plaintiffs' tax assessments. Although defendant did not exhaust its administrative remedies before filing suit, defendant presented a good-faith argument and belief that such was not necessary. Thus, damages were improperly awarded to plaintiffs.

 

98 of 1793 DOCUMENTS


Powers v. Palacios


No. D-0371


SUPREME COURT OF TEXAS


813 S.W.2d 489; 1991 Tex. LEXIS 96; 34 Tex. Sup. J. 651



June 12, 1991 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] From Nueces County, Thirteenth District.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff appealed a decision from the Nueces County, Thirteenth District Court (Texas), which entered judgment for defendant in plaintiff's personal injury action.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff initiated a personal injury action against defendant for injuries that she suffered due to a pit bull attack. During voir dire, defendant's attorney used his peremptory challenges to strike a potential juror. Plaintiff sought to question opposing counsel regarding his use of a peremptory challenge to exclude a juror based on race. The trial court overruled plaintiff's request and eventually entered judgment for defendant. Plaintiff contended opposing counsel had discriminatorily exercised a peremptory challenge. On appeal, the court reversed the decision and remanded for a new trial. The prohibition against using peremptory challenges to exclude jurors based on their race was extended to private litigants in civil actions. Previously, the prohibition was only upheld in criminal proceedings. The court reasoned that racial discrimination had no place in the judicial system, so that the use of race to exclude a potential juror was constitutionally prohibited.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision and remanded for further proceedings. Defendant's attorney improperly used a peremptory challenge to exclude a potential juror based on the juror's race. The prohibition against such action, which was only upheld in criminal actions, was extended to private litigants in civil proceedings.

Railroad Com. of Texas v. Pend Oreille Oil & Gas Co.


No. C-9937


SUPREME COURT OF TEXAS


817 S.W.2d 36; 1991 Tex. LEXIS 66; 34 Tex. Sup. J. 616; 113 Oil & Gas Rep. 573



June 5, 1991, Delivered


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] From Live Oak County; Thirteenth District.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner Railroad Commission sought review of a decision by the Court of Appeals (Texas), which reversed a petitioner's decision. The case involved the application of the Mineral Interest Pooling Act, Tex. Nat. Res. Code Ann. §§ 102.001-.112 (1978) by petitioner to a pooled gas unit. Respondent interest owner, who was force pooled, appealed petitioner commission's decision through the administrative appeals process to the court of appeals.


OVERVIEW: Respondent interest owner, who was force pooled under a decision by petitioner Railroad Commission, sought review of the decision through the administrative appeals process to the court of appeals. The court reversed in part, affirmed in part and partially vacated the district court's judgment, which affirmed petitioner commission's order. The issues in the case were whether the Mineral Interest Pooling Act, Tex. Nat. Res. Code Ann. §§ 102.001-.112 (1978) (MIPA) applicant made a fair and reasonable offer to pool voluntarily, whether petitioner commission could force pool, in a single MIPA proceeding, separate deposits of gas in man-made communication through a common well bore, and whether petitioner commission could enter a final order in a MIPA proceeding that established an effective date which is coincident with the date of an earlier interim order entered in the same proceeding. The reviewing court answered the questions in the affirmative. The court held that petitioner commission's finding that a corporation's offer to pool voluntarily was supported by substantial evidence, and that the court of appeals erred in reversing the order pooling two sands as a common reservoir.


OUTCOME: The court of appeals' judgment was affirmed in part and reversed in part. The court held that petitioner commission's finding that a corporation's offer to pool voluntarily was supported by substantial evidence, and that the court of appeals erred in reversing the order that pooled two sands as a common reservoir. The court also held that the court of appeals properly found that a corporation had invoked petitioner commission's jurisdiction.

Caulley v. Caulley


No. C-9274


SUPREME COURT OF TEXAS


806 S.W.2d 795; 1991 Tex. LEXIS 27; 34 Tex. Sup. J. 417



March 6, 1991, Delivered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Concurring Opinion Dated April 24, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY: From Harris County; Fourteenth District.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner ex-husband sought review of a judgment of the Fourteenth District Court of Appeals, Harris County (Texas), which affirmed an order reissuing a writ of execution on property on a money judgment for alimony and ordered petitioner to turn over a portion of his monthly paychecks to satisfy the judgment against him.


OVERVIEW: Subsequent to the parties' divorce, respondent ex-wife obtained a judgment for alimony and a writ of execution on the judgment against rural property petitioner ex-husband owned. Petitioner argued that the lower appellate court erred in determining a homestead exemption was not effective on the rural property. The court held the lower appellate court correctly determined the homestead exemption did not apply to the rural property, because petitioner and his new wife spent the majority of their time at other property for which petitioner's new wife had previously declared a homestead exemption. However, the court held that the trial court erred in ordering petitioner to turn over his wages to a receiver until the judgment against him had been satisfied, because Tex. Civ. Prac. & Rem. Code Ann. § 31.002(f) exempted paychecks from such turnover orders. In so holding, the court noted that the effective date of § 31.002(f) was subsequent to issuance of the turnover order, but the court held that the statute applied retroactively.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order reissuing a writ of execution on property owned by petitioner ex-husband for a money judgment of alimony to respondent ex-wife because the property was not covered by a homestead exemption. However, the court reversed the order requiring petitioner to turn over a portion of his wages to satisfy the judgment.

Guardian Royal Exchange Assur., Ltd. v. English China Clays, P.L.C.


No. C-8367


SUPREME COURT OF TEXAS


815 S.W.2d 223; 1991 Tex. LEXIS 19; 34 Tex. Sup. J. 376; 59 U.S.L.W. 2547



February 27, 1991, Delivered


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] From Gonzales County; Thirteenth District.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner insurer sought review of the judgment of the Court of Appeals (Texas), which reversed the trial court's judgment which granted petitioner's special appearance pursuant to Tex. R. Civ. P. 120a and dismissed, for lack of personal jurisdiction, the subrogation action filed by respondent insured corporations on behalf of their insurer.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner insurer was an English company with its principal place of business in England. Respondent insured corporations were American subsidiaries of an English company insured by petitioner. Pursuant to a policy issued by petitioner to the English company, respondents obtained liability insurance from an American insurer. Later, on behalf of the American insurer, respondents sued petitioner in state court for reimbursement of money paid to settle the wrongful death suits brought by the family of a deceased employee. The trial court granted petitioner's special appearance and dismissed the suit for lack of personal jurisdiction. On appeal, the intermediate appellate court reversed and remanded the case. On further appeal, the trial court's judgment was affirmed. The forum state's interests in adjudicating the dispute were minimal. The negotiation and performance of the policy at issue occurred in England. The real party in interest was the American insurer. Neither the deceased employee's family nor respondents had an interest in the suit. The assertion of personal jurisdiction over petitioner was unreasonable and did not comport with fair play and substantial justice.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the intermediate appellate court and affirmed the trial court's judgment, which granted petitioner insurer's special appearance and dismissed, for lack of personal jurisdiction, the subrogation action filed by respondent insured corporations on behalf of their insurer. It was inconsistent with federal constitutional requirements of due process to assert personal jurisdiction over petitioner.

Edgewood Indep. Sch. Dist. v. Kirby


No. D-0378


SUPREME COURT OF TEXAS


804 S.W.2d 491; 1991 Tex. LEXIS 21; 34 Tex. Sup. J. 368



February 25, 1991, Delivered


PRIOR HISTORY:  [*1] On Direct Appeal from a Judgment of the 250th District Court and on Application for Enforcement of Mandate.

Original Opinion of January 22, 1991, Reported at 1991 Tex. LEXIS 8.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, school district and others, moved for a rehearing of the court's judgment on an appeal of an order of the 250th District Court (Texas) in proceedings disputing the constitutionality of a school finance system.


OVERVIEW: In a dispute over a school system financing plan, plaintiffs, school district and others, sought an interpretation of the court's case law that would permit the state-wide recapture of local ad valorem tax revenue. On a motion for rehearing, the court noted that it had held that school districts could be organized along county or other lines and be given the authority to generate local property tax revenue for all of the other school districts within their boundaries. However, the court declined plaintiffs' argument that all school districts were mere creatures of the state, holding instead that Tex. Const. art. VIII, § 1-e, § 3 mandated that local tax revenue was not subject to state-wide recapture. The court then noted that the school funding system at issue was unconstitutional because the state relied on unequalized funding in an attempt to discharge its duty to support an educational system.


OUTCOME: The court denied the motion for rehearing by plaintiffs, school district and others, in a dispute over the constitutionality over a school funding system, because the funding system at issue was unconstitutional.

 

103 of 1793 DOCUMENTS


North Alamo Water Supply Corp. v. Willacy County Appraisal Dist.


Nos. C-8789, C-9525


SUPREME COURT OF TEXAS


804 S.W.2d 894; 1991 Tex. LEXIS 18; 34 Tex. Sup. J. 352



February 20, 1991, Delivered


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] From Willacy County, Thirteenth District; From Hidalgo County; Thirteenth District.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, non-profit water supply corporations, sought review of the judgments of the Thirteenth Court of Appeals (Texas), which affirmed judgments in favor of respondents, county appraisal districts and appraisal review boards, that petitioners were not purely public charities entitled to exemptions from ad valorem taxation under Tex. Tax Code Ann. §11.18.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners, non-profit water supply corporations, sought exemptions from ad valorem taxes as charitable organizations pursuant to Tex. Tax Code Ann. § 11.18(d)(7). Respondents, county appraisal districts, denied the applications and petitioners filed notices of protest with respondents, appraisal review boards, which also denied the applications. Petitioners filed suits challenging the denials. Both suits were tried on stipulated facts and resulted in judgments being entered against petitioners and the appeals were consolidated. The court held that only purely public charities were qualified to receive tax exemptions under Tex. Const. art. VIII, § 2. The court held that an organization must first meet applicable constitutional requirements before the organization can be considered for tax exempt status under Tex. Tax Code Ann. § 11.18. Because petitioners were not organized to provide charitable services to the community as a whole but were organized solely for the purpose of selling water to those within the boundaries of the district who could afford to pay the costs assessed, petitioners were not purely public charities entitled to exemption.


OUTCOME: Judgments that petitioners, non-profit water supply corporations, were not purely public charities entitled to exemptions from ad valorem taxation were affirmed because petitioners were not organized to provide charitable services to the community as a whole but were organized solely for the purpose of selling water to those within the boundaries of the district who could afford to pay the costs assessed.

 

104 of 1793 DOCUMENTS


Dickson v. Simpson


No. C-9521


SUPREME COURT OF TEXAS


807 S.W.2d 726; 1991 Tex. LEXIS 15; 34 Tex. Sup. J. 337



February 13, 1991, Delivered


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] From Lee County; Third District.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, illegitimate daughter, sought review of the judgment of a court of appeals (Texas), which affirmed the judgment of the trial court that held that petitioner's action to establish paternal inheritance rights was barred by the four-year general statute of limitations set forth in Tex. Civ. Prac. & Rem. Code Ann. § 16.051.


OVERVIEW: Deceased's spouse sought to probate the will. Petitioner, illegitimate daughter, contested the will on grounds that she was deceased's illegitimate daughter and asserted a right to parental inheritance. The trial court concluded that petitioner's action was barred by the four-year statute of limitations of Tex. Civ. Prac. & Rem. Code Ann. § 16.051. The court of appeals affirmed. Petitioner challenged the judgment, arguing that the statute of limitations unjustifiably discriminated against her and violated her right to equal protection under U.S. Const. amend. XIV, § 1. The court held that a statutory classification based on illegitimacy violated equal protection unless it was substantially related to an important governmental interest. The court held that the statutes here were not substantially related to the furtherance of the orderly administration of estates and effected a complete bar to petitioner's recovery regardless of her claim's merits. The court held that U.S. Const. amend. XIV, guaranteed petitioner a fair chance to establish paternity.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the court of appeals, which had affirmed the trial court's judgment that held that appellant illegitimate daughter's action to establish paternity inheritance rights was barred by the statute of limitations, and remanded for further proceedings. The equal protection clause guaranteed petitioner a fair opportunity to establish her heirship.

Edgewood Indep. Sch. Dist. v. Kirby


No. D-0378


SUPREME COURT OF TEXAS


804 S.W.2d 491; 1991 Tex. LEXIS 8; 34 Tex. Sup. J. 287



January 22, 1991, Delivered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Rehearing Overruled February 25, 1991, Reported at 1991 Tex. LEXIS 21.


PRIOR HISTORY: On Direct Appeal from a Judgment of the 250th District Court and on Application for Enforcement of Mandate.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs sought relief from a judgment of the 250th District Court and for enforcement of mandate on the basis that the district court exceeded its authority by vacating the court's injunction. Defendant state officials cross-appealed on the basis that the district court erred in finding that school finance system continued to violate Tex. Const. art. VII, § 1 after enactment of corrective legislation.


OVERVIEW: The court directed the district court to vacate that portion of a judgment that vacated an injunction affirmed by the court in an underlying action. The injunction enjoined defendants, state commissioner of education, state board of education, and state comptroller, from distributing any money under the then current Texas School Financing System, Tex. Educ. Code Ann. §16.01, § et. seq., until the legislature passed a constitutionally sufficient plan. The court held that the district court correctly concluded as a matter of law that corrective legislation enacted in response to the court's injunction continued to violate Tex. Const. art. VII, § 1, as it left essentially intact the same funding system and failed to provide a direct and close correlation between a district's tax effort and the educational resources available to it. The court held that the district court abused its discretion in refusing to enforce the mandate of the court on equitable grounds and to avoid disruption to public education, as only the court could decide that for policy reasons its mandate should have been modified or vacated.


OUTCOME: The district court was directed to vacate the portion of its judgment that vacated the injunction affirmed by the court and not to extend the modified deadline or to modify the injunction. The court held that based on the district court's finding that corrective legislation continued an unconstitutional funding system for public education, the district court clearly abused its discretion in refusing to enforce the mandate of the court.

Orange County v. Ware


No. C-9611


SUPREME COURT OF TEXAS


1990 Tex. LEXIS 155; 34 Tex. Sup. J. 252



December 31, 1990, Delivered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [*1] Dissenting Opinion Dated January 23, 1991. Rehearing Denied November 20, 1991, Reported at 1991 Tex. LEXIS 143. Withdrawn November 20, 1991; Substituted Opinion Reported at 1991 Tex. LEXIS 143.


PRIOR HISTORY: From the 260th District Court of Orange County, Texas.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant county challenged the ruling of the District Court of Orange County (Texas) which temporarily enjoined appellant from withholding the salary of appellee commissioner.


OVERVIEW: Appellant county challenged the order of the trial court which enjoined it and its county attorney and county auditor from withholding part of the salary of appellee county commissioner, on the grounds that the statute authorizing such withholding constituted an unconstitutional garnishment of his wages. Appellee argued that, according to Tex. Const. art. XVI, § 28, appellant county wrongfully garnished his wages. The trial court also held that appellant county's action taken pursuant to Tex. Loc. Gov't Code Ann. § 154.025 were in direct conflict with Tex. Const. art. XVI, § 28. The court held that garnishment had to involve a third party and that appellant and its agents were not collecting amounts owed to a third party. Therefore, the statutory provision for withholding warrants when a party was indebted to a subdivision of the state as a matter of law was not a garnishment. Based on this reasoning, the court vacated the order granting a temporary injunction and rendered judgment denying temporary relief.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the ruling of the trial court which held that appellant county violated the state constitution by garnishing the wages of appellee. The court reasoned that when a party was indebted to a subdivision of the state, the withholding of wages was not garnishment as a matter of law.

Travis v. Mesquite


No. C-8576


SUPREME COURT OF TEXAS


1990 Tex. LEXIS 158; 34 Tex. Sup. J. 231



December 31, 1990, Delivered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [*1] Motion for Rehearing Overruled May 20, 1992, Reported at 1990 Tex. LEXIS 158.


PRIOR HISTORY: From Dallas County, Fifth District.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, parent as next friend of decedent and minor children plus survivors on behalf of other decedent's estate, challenged a judgment of the Court of Appeals, Dallas County (Texas), Fifth District, that affirmed a summary judgment from the trial court in favor of respondents, City of Mesquite, Texas, and four police officers, that the respondents police officers' actions could not constitute a proximate cause of a fatal pursuit accident.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners, parent as next friend of decedent and minor children and survivors on behalf of other decedent's estate, sued respondents, City of Mesquite, Texas, and police officers for negligence, gross negligence, and violations of their civil rights under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 (1982). The court reversed the court of appeals judgment that affirmed the trial court and remanded the cause as to the two respondent police officers instituting the pursuit and the respondent city. The summary judgment evidence raised the fact issue of whether the decision to pursue, under the facts and circumstances, was a proximate cause of the accident. The court affirmed the court of appeals judgment that affirmed the trial court summary judgment for the two respondent officers who responded to the radio call for assistance. The court ruled that Tex. Rev. Civ. Stat. Ann. art. 6701d, §§ 24, 74 (1989) neither excepted police officers from the legal standards for proximate cause nor shielded them officers from liability for accidents in pursuit. The court ruled that the qualified immunity doctrine of § 1983 was subject to limitations when the police activity created a danger to citizens.


OUTCOME: The court reversed summary judgment for respondents, City of Mesquite, Texas, and the two police officers that initiated the pursuit because the respondent police officers' actions could have constituted a proximate cause of the fatal pursuit accident. The court affirmed summary judgment in favor of the other two other police officers.

Cherokee Water Co. v. Gregg County Appraisal Dist.


No. C-9052


SUPREME COURT OF TEXAS


801 S.W.2d 872; 1990 Tex. LEXIS 157; 34 Tex. Sup. J. 239



December 31, 1990, Delivered


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] From Gregg County, Twelfth District.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent district made a value determination of petitioner taxpayer's property in order to assess ad valorem taxes. Petitioner sought review of the court of appeals of Gregg County, Twelfth District (Texas), which affirmed the new valuation of petitioner's property made by the trial court.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner taxpayer owned property which respondent district appraised in order to assess ad valorem taxes. Petitioner challenged the valuation and argued that the appraisal method was not applicable to petitioner's property. The trial court rendered a judgment, which set new valuations, and the court of appeals affirmed. The court affirmed the court of appeals. The court held that the appeal of respondent's appraisal was by trial de novo and that the trial court had the power to determine the value of petitioner's property. The court found that it did not know the specific method that the trial court used in arriving at its valuation and that it could not be disputed based on the method used by respondent's appraiser. The court found that the findings of fact and conclusions of law did not state what methods the trial court used in its valuation, and without such findings, the court could not say that the trial court's valuation was improper.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the court of appeals, which affirmed the trial court's valuation of petitioner taxpayer's property for the purpose of allowing respondent district to assess ad valorem taxes. The court determined that the findings of fact and conclusions of law did not state the specific method the trial court used in its valuation; therefore, the court could not say that the valuation was improper.

Rev. Civ. Stat. Ann. art. 4590i, §§ 11.02, 11.03 (1991). Respondent hospital complained of the lower court's reversal of the trial court's j.n.o.v. and rendition.


OVERVIEW: A jury awarded damages to petitioner family of decedent, but the trial court rendered judgment notwithstanding the verdict. The lower court reversed, holding that there was some evidence that respondent hospital caused decedent's death and suggested remittiturs to reduce the damages awarded by the jury in accordance with Tex. Rev. Civ. Stat. Ann. art. 4590i, § 11.02. Petitioners filed remittiturs, but expressly reserved the right to complain of the ruling. Respondent contended that the remittitur became fatally defective because petitioners reserved the right to complain of the standard used in arriving at it. The court held that the remittitur was unaffected by a reservation of the right to appeal the standard and the correct standard was applied. The court reversed the judgment below insofar as it remanded for a new trial and otherwise affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the lower court insofar as it remanded for a new trial and affirmed in all other respects.

Rose v. Doctors Hosp. Facilities


No. C-6535


SUPREME COURT OF TEXAS


1990 Tex. LEXIS 111; 33 Tex. Sup. J. 672



September 6, 1990, Delivered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [*1] As Revised. Withdrawn December 19, 1990; Substituted Opinion Reported at 1990 Tex. LEXIS 153. Rehearing Granted in Part and Overruled in Part December 19, 1990.


PRIOR HISTORY: From Dallas County Fifth District.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners wife and parents of deceased sought review of an order from the Court of Appeals (Texas) which suggested remittiturs reducing the damages of petitioner wife in accordance with the Medical Liability and Insurance Improvement Act, Tex. Rev. Civ. Stat. Ann. art. 4590i, § 11.02 (Supp. 1990), and those of petitioner parents to an amount supported by the evidence, in petitioners' wrongful death action against respondent hospital.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners wife and parents of deceased filed a wrongful death action against respondent hospital relating to deceased's death. At trial, the jury awarded damages to petitioners, but the trial judge rendered judgment notwithstanding the verdict. Petitioners sought relief, and the appellate court reversed, finding that there was evidence that respondent caused deceased's death, and suggested remittiturs reducing petitioners' damages in accordance with the Medical Liability and Insurance Improvement Act, Tex. Rev. Civ. Stat. Ann. art. 4590i, §§ 11.02 and 11.03 (Supp. 1990) (Medical Liability Act). Petitioners filed remittiturs and sought relief from the appellate court's order. The court affirmed. The Medical Liability Act was valid and could be applied to wrongful death claims that did not include any common law claims in order to reduce the damages. The Medical Liability Act, further, did not violate equal protection under U.S. Const. amend. XIV § 1 or Tex. Const. art. I, § 3, and petitioners' remittiturs did not restrict or destroy petitioners' right to appeal.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed and held that the Medical Liability and Insurance Improvement Act was valid and could be applied to wrongful death claims that did not include any common law claims, that it could be applied to reduce the damages, that it did not violate equal protection under the Fourteenth Amendment or the Texas constitution, and that the petitioners' remittiturs did not restrict or destroy petitioners' right to appeal.

 

112 of 1793 DOCUMENTS


Weatherford v. Parker County


No. C-9217


SUPREME COURT OF TEXAS


794 S.W.2d 33; 1990 Tex. LEXIS 104; 33 Tex. Sup. J. 651



July 3, 1990, Delivered


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] From Parker County, Second District.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxing unit appealed from the decision by the Second District Court, Parker County (Texas), which affirmed the trial court's judgment that an election, held by defendant county pursuant to Tex. Tax Code Ann. § 6.26, was not binding on plaintiff. Plaintiff sought affirmation that § 6.26 impermissibly exceeded the authority granted to the legislature by Tex. Const. art. III, § 64(a).


OVERVIEW: Defendant county conducted an election, which consolidated the functions of several taxing units, pursuant to procedures allowed by Tex. Tax Code Ann. § 6.26. The outcome of the election was contested by plaintiff taxing unit in a declaratory judgment action, seeking judgment that § 6.26 was unconstitutional, and that the election was therefore without effect. The trial court held that § 6.26 impermissibly exceeded the authority granted to the legislature by Tex. Const. art. III, § 64(a). On first appeal, the district court concluded that the election was not binding upon plaintiff taxing unit, and affirmed the judgment of the trial court to that extent. However, the court of appeals did not conclude that § 6.26 otherwise conflicted with the state constitution, and held that the election was binding on all of the other taxing units affected. Plaintiff appealed for a judgment on the constitutionality of § 6.26. The court held that § 6.26 was unconstitutional, as it authorized by general statute action which, under Tex. Const. art. III, § 64(a) could be authorized only by special statute. The court declared the election a nullity.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment for plaintiff taxing unit, and expanded on the affirmation of the court of appeals, holding that the tax statute which permitted consolidation of taxing units for election purposes was unconstitutional, as it authorized by general statute action which could only be authorized by special statute. It declared the election conducted by defendant county a nullity on the basis of its holding.

Brady v. Fourteenth Court of Appeals


No. C-9538


SUPREME COURT OF TEXAS


795 S.W.2d 712; 1990 Tex. LEXIS 95; 33 Tex. Sup. J. 609

PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Original Mandamus Proceeding.
