Cantu v. State


NO. 71,857


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF TEXAS


939 S.W.2d 627; 1997 Tex. Crim. App. LEXIS 118



January 29, 1997, DELIVERED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] 
Certiorari Denied December 1, 1997, Reported at: 1997 U.S. LEXIS 7072.
Writ of habeas corpus denied Ex parte Cantu, 2006 Tex. Crim. App. Unpub. LEXIS 292 (Tex. Crim. App., Dec. 13, 2006)
Writ of habeas corpus denied, Summary judgment granted by, Dismissed by, Certificate of appealability denied Cantu v. Quarterman, 2009 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 8252 (S.D. Tex., Feb. 4, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from HARRIS County.
Cantu v. State, 1996 Tex. Crim. App. LEXIS 259 (Tex. Crim. App., 1997)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment entered in Harris County (Texas), which sentenced defendant to death after defendant was found guilty of capital murder for killing a victim in the course of committing or attempting to commit robbery, kidnapping, and aggravated sexual assault.


OVERVIEW: The court affirmed the trial court's decision, which found defendant guilty of capital murder and sentenced defendant to death. Defendant was the self-appointed leader of a gang that raped and killed two girls; defendant instructed others in the gang to strangle the girls and personally, among other acts, kicked one of the victims in the face knocking out several of her teeth and stepped on the neck of the other victim until she stopped moving. Defendant appealed on forty-five points of error, which included contentions that jury instructions and applied statutory law were improper and unconstitutional, inadmissible evidence was admitted, a victim impact statement was improperly admitted, the state advanced an improper argument, and the death penalty as administered in the state was unconstitutional. The court overruled all points of error and held that, pursuant to common law, a defendant was not entitled to an instruction on parole law in a capital case. The court also held that, though the victim impact statement was improperly admitted, the admission was harmless beyond a reasonable doubt. The court held that all other evidence and instructions were proper and constitutional.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's conviction of defendant of capital murder and sentence of death on the grounds that defendant was not entitled to a jury instruction of parole law, and that the applicable statutory law and evidence admitted was constitutional and admissible or resulted in a harmless error.

Ex parte Coleman


NO. 553-96


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF TEXAS


940 S.W.2d 96; 1996 Tex. Crim. App. LEXIS 250



December 18, 1996, Delivered


PRIOR HISTORY:   [**1] Petition for Discretionary Review from the First Court of Appeals. [HARRIS County].



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state filed a petition for discretionary review of a determination by the First Court of Appeals, Harris County (Texas), that the subsequent prosecution of appellant on a second indictment alleging theft was barred by double jeopardy.


OVERVIEW: A grand jury indicted appellant for theft in an indictment which alleged that the name of the property owner was unknown at that time. At the end of the jury trial, appellant's motion for instructed verdict was granted. The state's second indictment which named the property owner was determined to be jeopardy barred by the lower court. The court noted that the state was attempting to prosecute appellant for the same violation of the same penal statute in separate trials with the only difference being the naming of a complainant. The court held that because the evidence relied on by the state at the second trial would be the same as that presented at the first trial, the offenses alleged in each indictment were the same and consequently, the second trial was jeopardy barred. The court affirmed the judgment of the lower court.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the determination that the subsequent prosecution of appellant was jeopardy barred because once the state went to trial on an indictment alleging an unknown property owner and jeopardy attached, the state could not retry appellant for the same violation of the same statute by alleging the name of the property owner.

Ex parte Davis


No. 72,247


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF TEXAS


947 S.W.2d 216; 1996 Tex. Crim. App. LEXIS 256



December 18, 1996, DELIVERED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As Corrected October 1, 1997.


PRIOR HISTORY: Habeas corpus application from TRAVIS County.



DISPOSITION: Therefore, our order of January 2, 1996 granting a stay of execution is hereby vacated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Applicant filed with the court (Texas) a subsequent application for post-conviction writ of habeas corpus.


OVERVIEW: Applicant filed a second application for post-conviction writ of habeas corpus and claimed that Tex. Code Crim. Proc. Ann. art. 11.071, § 5's provisions (provisions) for subsequent applications for writ of habeas corpus were unconstitutional. Article 11.071, § 5 provided that a court could not consider subsequent applications unless they contained sufficient specific facts establishing various elements. The court held that the provisions did not violate the separation of powers clause or suspend certain rights because it merely provided the methodology for rendering the writ and did not prevent the court from exercising its powers. Because the provisions were procedural and did not disturb substantive rights, the court held they were outside the "retroactive law" prohibition. Because the provisions applied all felons, the court held they did not violate equal protection. The court held the provisions did not violate due process or due course of law merely because the requirements were stringent. The court overruled applicant's grounds of error, dismissed his application as an abuse of writ, and vacated the order granting applicant a stay of execution.


OUTCOME: The court overruled applicant's grounds of error, finding that provisions for subsequent applications for writ of habeas corpus were constitutional. The court dismissed his application as an abuse of writ because it did not meet the requirements of such provisions, and vacated the order granting applicant a stay of execution.

 

159 of 1793 DOCUMENTS


Williams v. State


NO. 72,128


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF TEXAS


937 S.W.2d 479; 1996 Tex. Crim. App. LEXIS 254



December 18, 1996, DELIVERED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing overruled by, 01/29/1997
Writ of habeas corpus denied Williams v. Cockrell, 31 Fed. Appx. 832, 2002 U.S. App. LEXIS 2127 (5th Cir. Tex., 2002)


PRIOR HISTORY:   [**1] Appeal from HARRIS County.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, following his arrest after a robbery, was convicted of murder and sentenced to death in a Harris County court (Texas). Defendant appealed automatically under Tex. Code Crim. Proc. Ann. art. 37.071 § 2(h) and raised 24 points of error including two on insufficient legal evidence, one on his motion to quash indictment, 10 on guilt/innocence, and 11 on punishment.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was arrested, tried, and convicted for murder and robbery in a residential apartment and sentenced to death. Following a death penalty sentence, defendant automatically appealed pursuant to Tex. Code Crim. Proc. Ann. art. 37.071 § 2(h), alleging more than 20 points of error at trial, of which nearly all related to either guilt/innocence or punishment. The court found most points lacking adequate legal argument or citing incorrect legal standards and affirmed the conviction and sentencing. Reviewing the evidence in a light most favorable to the verdict the court found it sufficient for a rational jury to answer "yes" to defendant's future dangerousness and also to infer that defendant intended to steal before the murder. The state had to prove the latter in a capital murder prosecution and the former before submitting death penalty appropriateness to the jury. The court rejected the unconstitutionality claim that state had no burden to prove aggravating circumstances and held that Texas required proof of both the death penalty-crime and future dangerousness before submitting the appropriateness question to the jury including consideration of mitigating circumstances.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's murder conviction and death sentence and found that a rational jury could have found defendant formed the intent to rob and that he probably would constitute a continuing threat to society. All of defendant's points on guilt/innocence and punishment were overruled for insufficient or inaccurate legal arguments.

Jones v. State


NO. 72,026


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF TEXAS


944 S.W.2d 642; 1996 Tex. Crim. App. LEXIS 251



December 18, 1996, Delivered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Appellant's Motion for Rehearing Denied February 19, 1997. Certiorari Denied October 6, 1997, Reported at: 1997 U.S. LEXIS 4921.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from GREGG County.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed the judgment of the trial court.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: An automatic appeal was pursued by defendant of a judgment from Gregg County Court (Texas), in which he was convicted of murder in the course of robbery under Tex. Penal Code Ann. § 19.03(a)(2), and the death penalty was imposed pursuant to Tex. Code Crim. Proc. Ann. art. 37.071, § 2(b), (e), and (h).


OVERVIEW: Defendant fired a gun and killed the victim but claimed it was an accident. Defendant was convicted of the offense of capital murder, specifically murder in the course of robbery under Tex. Penal Code § 19.03(a)(2). The jury found defendant guilty and imposed the death penalty pursuant to Tex. Code Crim. Proc. Ann. art. 37.071, § 2(b), (e), and (h). Appeal was automatic and the court affirmed the trial court's judgment. The court held that the evidence showed defendant planned the robbery and brought with him a deadly weapon in order to accomplish the task. The court determined that a rational trier of fact could have found beyond a reasonable doubt that defendant intentionally caused the death of the deceased. Although defendant presented probative evidence showing that he did not intentionally shoot the victim, there existed other probative evidence that he did. The court held that the jury's finding that defendant intentionally killed the victim was not so contrary to the overwhelming weight of the evidence as to be clearly wrong or unjust. Therefore, the court deferred to the jury's conclusion that the evidence was not sufficient to warrant a sentence of life imprisonment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, which convicted defendant and sentenced him to death because the court found no reversible error. The court held that there was no reason to conclude that the jury's verdict was manifestly unjust, shocked the conscience, or clearly demonstrated bias.

State v. Roberts


NO. 1126-95


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF TEXAS


940 S.W.2d 655; 1996 Tex. Crim. App. LEXIS 245



December 11, 1996, Delivered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As Corrected December 18, 1996. As Corrected April 22, 1997.


PRIOR HISTORY: Petition for Discretionary Review from the Fourteenth Court of Appeals. [HARRIS County].



DISPOSITION: Judgment of Court of Appeals vacated; cause remanded to Court of Appeals.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state petitioned for discretionary review of a decision from the Fourteenth Court of Appeals (Texas), which affirmed the granting of appellee's motion to exclude the complainant's testimony in a civil deposition. The state had charged appellee with misapplication of fiduciary property under Tex. Penal Code Ann. § 32.45.


OVERVIEW: Appellee filed a pretrial motion in a prosecution for misapplication of fiduciary property under Tex. Penal Code Ann. § 32.45 requesting the exclusion of the complainant's civil deposition testimony. The trial court granting of the motion was affirmed when the state appealed pursuant to Tex. Code Crim. Proc. Ann. art. 44.01(a)(5). The court vacated the judgment of the lower court with instructions to dismiss the appeal for want of jurisdiction. The court held that the phrase "motion to suppress evidence" as used in art. 44.01(a)(5) was limited to motions which sought to suppress evidence on the basis that such evidence was illegally obtained. Appellee did not contend the deposition testimony was illegally secured. The court determined that the order granting the motion was not appealable because the motion was not a "motion to suppress evidence" as contemplated under art. 44.01(a)(5).


OUTCOME: The court vacated the judgment of the lower court and remanded the case with instructions to dismiss the appeal for want of jurisdiction because the order granting the motion was not appealable.

Shannon v. State


NO. 71,805


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF TEXAS


942 S.W.2d 591; 1996 Tex. Crim. App. LEXIS 247



December 11, 1996, Delivered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by, 01/29/1997
Post-conviction relief denied at Shannon v. State, 2003 Tex. Crim. App. LEXIS 112 (Tex. Crim. App., June 18, 2003)


PRIOR HISTORY:   [**1] Appeal from HARRIS County.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Automatic direct appeal from the judgment of the Harris County Court (Texas), which convicted defendant of capital murder.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of capital murder and sentenced to death. The court affirmed. The court held that defendant's requested "parole ineligible" instruction was appropriately refused by the trial court since parole is not a proper area of inquiry in Texas capital cases. The court found defendant failed to make a timely objection to jury's note-taking, but the trial judge did not abuse his discretion in allowing the jurors to take individual notes. The court held that Tex. Code Crim. Proc. Ann. art. 37.071 § 2(f)(4) called for the consideration and weighing of mitigating evidence and did not unconstitutionally narrow the jury's discretion to factors concerning only moral blameworthiness. The state's reference to defendant as a sociopath was improper, but not so egregious as to constitute reversible error and the prompt instruction to disregard cured any error. The court held that the capital murder scheme did not amount to cruel and unusual punishment, in violation of U.S. Const. amends. VIII & XIV, because the class of murderers subject to the death penalty is already narrowed before a jury ever reaches the art. 37.071 § 2(e) instruction.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant capital murder conviction and death sentence. The court held that the state's capital murder scheme did not amount to cruel and unusual punishment and the trial court's evidentiary rulings were upheld.

Janecka v. State


NO. 71,803


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF TEXAS


937 S.W.2d 456; 1996 Tex. Crim. App. LEXIS 240



November 27, 1996, Delivered  
November 27, 1996, filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As Corrected April 22, 1997. Certiorari Denied October 6, 1997, Reported at: 1997 U.S. LEXIS 4825.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from 248th District Court of Harris County.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant automatically appealed, pursuant to Tex. Code Crim. Proc. Ann. art. 37.0711, § 3(j), his murder conviction and mandatory death sentence entered in the 248th District Court of Harris County (Texas).


OVERVIEW: A jury convicted defendant of murder under Tex. Penal Code Ann. § 19.03(a)(3). The trial court sentenced defendant to death pursuant to Tex. Crim. Proc. Code Ann. art. 37.0711 § 3(g) after the jury answered the special issues submitted under Tex. Crim. Proc. Code Ann. art. 37.0711 § 3(b)(1) & (2) and § 3(e). Defendant's appeal was automatic pursuant to Tex. Crim. Proc. Code Ann. art. 37.0711 § 3(j). Defendant raised issues related to his confession, the search warrant, right to counsel, and limitations on jury voir dire. The court held that eliminating the state's duty to refute defendant's exculpatory remarks in his confession did not violate due process. There was no material misrepresentation in the affidavit supporting the search warrant where the identity and the criminal history of the informant was obscured. Defendant's confession, obtained while he was in custody for an unrelated offense, was not a violation of his right to counsel. The trial court's limitation on jury voir dire was harmless because defendant failed to exercise all of his preemptory challenges. The court affirmed the conviction, and held that defendant's numerous other issues were held to be without merit.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction and death sentence. The trial court limitation on jury voir dire was harmless when defendant did not exercise all of his preemptory challenges. In addition, obscuring the identity of an informant was permissible to obtain the search warrant and the resulting evidence was admissible.

Eldridge v. State


NO. 71,863


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF TEXAS


940 S.W.2d 646; 1996 Tex. Crim. App. LEXIS 235



November 20, 1996, Delivered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Writ of habeas corpus dismissed Ex parte Eldridge, 2005 Tex. Crim. App. Unpub. LEXIS 402 (Tex. Crim. App., Feb. 9, 2005)
Writ of habeas corpus denied Ex parte Eldridge, 2005 Tex. Crim. App. Unpub. LEXIS 403 (Tex. Crim. App., Feb. 9, 2005)
Habeas corpus proceeding at Eldridge v. Quarterman, 2007 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 1259 (S.D. Tex., Jan. 5, 2007)
Decision reached on appeal by Eldridge v. State, 2009 Tex. Crim. App. Unpub. LEXIS 840 (Tex. Crim. App., Nov. 16, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:   [**1] Appeal from 178th District Court of HARRIS County.



DISPOSITION: Finding no reversible error, we affirmed the judgment of the trial court.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the conviction for capital murder from the 178th District Court of Harris County (Texas), as it related to the special issues at the punishment phase of the trial.


OVERVIEW: Appellant challenged the punishment phase of his conviction for capital murder. The court affirmed the conviction of the lower court and held that while Tex. Code Crim. Proc. Ann. art. 37.071(2)(a) prohibited any mention in a capital murder trial of the effect of a failure of the jury to agree on either of the special issues submitted to it, the jury instruction given on special issues attenuated any harm. The court held that the lower court's refusal to instruct the jury on the parole implications of a life sentence did not violate U.S. Const. amend. VIII or U.S. Const. amend. XIV because parole ineligibility did not tend to demonstrate evidence of future dangerousness and was neither in the jury charge nor a permissible subject of final argument. The court held that sufficiency reviews were made of juries' guilt/innocence and future dangerousness decisions as the decisions were fact bound. However, no appellate review was required of the jury determination whether evidence had mitigating effect and, if so, how much under Tex. Code Crim. Proc. Ann. art. 37.071 § 2(e), as those decisions were normative and therefore not reviewable.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the conviction of the lower court and held that sufficiency reviews were made of juries' guilt/innocence and future dangerousness decisions, however the jury determined whether any evidence had mitigating effect and, if so, how much and that those two decisions were not reviewable by the court.

Bell v. State


NO. 71,843


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF TEXAS


938 S.W.2d 35; 1996 Tex. Crim. App. LEXIS 232



November 20, 1996, Delivered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Certiorari Denied October 6, 1997, Reported at: 1997 U.S. LEXIS 4855.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Criminal District Court of JEFFERSON County.



DISPOSITION: Finding no reversible error, we affirmed the judgment of the trial court.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant automatically appealed his death sentence under Tex. Code Crim. Proc. Ann. art. 37.0711 § 3(j) from the Criminal District Court of Jefferson County (Texas). Defendant was convicted of capital murder pursuant to Tex. Penal Code Ann. § 19.03(a)(2). The jury answered the issues of Tex. Code Crim. Proc. Ann. art. 37.0711 §§ 3(b)(1) and (2) affirmatively; and the issue of Tex. Code Crim. Proc. Ann. art. 37.0711 § 3(e) negatively.


OVERVIEW: A jury convicted defendant of capital murder under Texas Penal Code Ann. § 19.03(a)(2). Defendant had committed murder in the course of a robbery. The trial court sentenced defendant to death pursuant to Tex. Code Crim. Proc. Code art. 37.0711 § 3(g), and appeal was automatic under Tex. Code Crim. Proc. Ann. art 37.0711 § 3(j). The court would not review the jury's answer to the mitigation special issue because it was a practical impossibility. Venue was proper because publicity about the trial did not foster a hostile public attitude against defendant. The court held that including defendant's death threats there was sufficient evidence of his future dangerousness. Defendant did not preserve his confession issue, because he had objected to his illegal arrest but not his confession. Defendant's mental retardation could be considered an aggravating factor. The trial court could allow a witness who violated the sequestration order to testify. The court held that the trial court could require the jury to deliberate further, after a total of nine hours and forty minutes. Affirming the judgment, the court concluded that there was no basis for defendant's constitutional challenges.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for capital murder and death sentence. There was sufficient evidence of defendant's future dangerousness and defendant did not preserve the issue regarding his confession because he objected to his arrest but not the resulting confession.

 

166 of 1793 DOCUMENTS


State v. Johnson


NO. 610-95


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF TEXAS


939 S.W.2d 586; 1996 Tex. Crim. App. LEXIS 233



November 20, 1996, Delivered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As Corrected November 20, 1996.


PRIOR HISTORY: Petition For Discretionary Review from the First Court of Appeals. 23rd District Court of Brazoria County.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state petitioned for discretionary review of an interlocutory appeal of the Houston Court of Appeals, First Supreme Judicial District (Texas), affirming a trial court's judgment granting defendant's motions to suppress evidence in violation of Tex. Code Crim. Proc. art. 38.23(a), an exclusionary rule. Defendant was charged with capital murder and alleged searches in, and removal of evidence from, his funeral home and hearse were illegal.


OVERVIEW: Defendant and decedent jointly operated and lived in the upstairs of a funeral home where decedent had allegedly been shot. Defendant was charged with capital murder. A trial court granted defendant's motions to suppress evidence in violation of Tex. Code Crim. Proc. art. 38.23(a), alleging searches in, and removal of evidence from, the funeral home and hearse were illegal. The appellate court affirmed the grant of the motions holding art. 38.23(a) extended to actions of private persons. The evidence decedent's sons turned over to the police from the funeral home, therefore, was suppressed. The court affirmed the appellate court's holdings on discretionary review, holding that the plain language of art. 38.23(a) controlled and supported the contention that the unlawful or unconstitutional actions of all people, governmental and private alike, fell under the state's exclusionary rule. The fruit of illegally obtained evidence on the subsequent search issue was proper, and the good faith exception to the exclusionary could not be addressed.


OUTCOME: On discretionary review, the court affirmed the appellate court's decision granting defendant's motions to suppress in a capital murder charge. Searches in, and removal of evidence from, a funeral home and hearse were illegal under an exclusionary rule, no exceptions were raised, the rule extended to private persons, and fruits of a subsequent search by a warrant based on illegal information were suppressed, against the state.

Matchett v. State


NO. 71,664


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF TEXAS


941 S.W.2d 922; 1996 Tex. Crim. App. LEXIS 220



November 6, 1996, Delivered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As Corrected November 7, 1996. Second Correction January 23, 1997. Third Correction May 9, 1997. Fourth Correction June 9, 1997. Certiorari Denied June 23, 1997, Reported at: 1997 U.S. LEXIS 3926 
Rehearing denied by, 01/22/1997
Writ of certiorari denied Matchett v. Texas, 521 U.S. 1107, 138 L. Ed. 2d 994, 117 S. Ct. 2487, 1997 U.S. LEXIS 3926 (1997)
Application denied by Matchett v. Dretke, 2004 U.S. App. LEXIS 16377 (5th Cir. Tex., Aug. 9, 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Harris County.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged a judgment of a Harris County trial court (Texas), which convicted appellant of robbery murder under Tex. Penal Code § 19.03(a)(2) after he entered a plea of guilty. The trial court sentenced appellant to death pursuant to Tex. Code Crim. Proc. Ann. art. 37.071(e) after the jury affirmatively answered the two special issues under Tex. Code Crim. Proc. Ann. art. 37.071(b).


OVERVIEW: Appellant challenged his robbery murder conviction and death sentence. The court held that the trial court was not in substantial compliance when it failed to admonish appellant under Tex. Code Crim. Proc. Ann. art. 26.13(a)(2)-(4). The error was harmless because the art. 26.13(a)(2) and (3) admonishments did not apply to a capital murder case in which death was sought, and the art. 26.13(a)(4) admonishment did not apply because appellant was a United States citizen. Appellant's plea in open court and on the record outside the presence of the jury satisfied Tex. Code Crim. Proc. Ann. art. 27.13. Appellant received a jury trial because the jury entered a verdict as to guilt and answered the special issues under Tex. Code Crim. Proc. Ann. art. 37.071. The trial court did not violate the separation of powers but merely assured that appellant was sentenced to death in accordance with law. The capital punishment scheme was not unconstitutional. The mitigating evidence instruction did not need to assign a burden of proof. Sufficient evidence supported the finding that appellant was a continuing threat to society because evidence showed a dangerous aberration of character.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment convicting appellant of robbery murder and sentencing him to death. Appellant's guilty plea was knowing and voluntary, and he received a jury trial because the jury entered the verdict of guilt and answered the statutory special issues. The jury instruction concerning the mitigating evidence special issue was proper. Sufficient evidence supported the finding that appellant was a continuing threat to society.

 

168 of 1793 DOCUMENTS


State v. Savage


NO. 866-95


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF TEXAS


933 S.W.2d 497; 1996 Tex. Crim. App. LEXIS 221



November 6, 1996, Delivered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As Corrected November 12, 1996.


PRIOR HISTORY: Petition for Discretionary Review from the Fourth Court of Appeals. [BEXAR County].



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellee defendant sought discretionary review of the judgment of the Fourth Court of Appeals, Bexar County (Texas), which reversed a trial court's judgment non obstante verdicto, which in effect was an order granting a new trial, and entered a conviction for driving while intoxicated against him.


OVERVIEW: A jury convicted appellee defendant of driving while intoxicated. At the trial court's behest, appellee moved for, and was granted, a judgment non obstante verdicto (JNOV), which in essence was a judgment granting a new trial. Appellant state appealed, and the lower court reversed and vacated the trial court's judgment, and entered a judgment of guilty. Appellee sought discretionary review of the lower court's action. On appeal, the court affirmed, holding that the trial court did not have the authority to grant a different judgment than that rendered by the jury, and that Tex. R. App. P. 80(b) allowed appellate courts to render the verdict that the court below should have rendered. In this case, because there was a jury verdict rendered, the lower court was authorized to overrule the trial court's judgment and reinstate the jury's verdict.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the court of appeals, holding that the trial court had no authority to grant a judgment non obstante verdicto in a criminal case and that the court of appeals had statutory authority to render the judgment the court below should have rendered, a judgment of conviction upon the jury's verdict.

Massey v. State


NO. 72,025


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF TEXAS


933 S.W.2d 141; 1996 Tex. Crim. App. LEXIS 217



October 23, 1996, Delivered  
October 23, 1996, filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Habeas corpus proceeding at, Application denied by Massey v. Johnson, 234 F.3d 29, 2000 U.S. App. LEXIS 26966 (5th Cir. Tex., 2000)


PRIOR HISTORY:   [**1] Appeal from Ellis County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of his conviction from a District Court of Ellis County (Texas) for capital murder under Tex. Penal Code Ann. § 19.03(a)(7)(A).


OVERVIEW: Defendant received automatic review under Tex. Code Crim. Proc. Ann. art 37.071, § 2(h) of his conviction for capital murder under Tex. Penal Code Ann. § 19.03(a)(7)(A). The court affirmed his conviction because defendant did not show that any alleged omissions in the affidavit used to secure a search warrant were made intentionally or for the purpose of misleading the magistrate. The court held that when looking at the four corners of the affidavit, it alleged facts sufficient to justify a conclusion that the property that was the object of the search probably was in defendant's residence or car. The court also held that the trial court did not commit an error in overruling defendant's challenges for cause to veniremembers who stated that they would not consider certain evidence as mitigating because only the individual juror could decide what mitigating weight, if any, would be given to particular evidence. The court held that a psychiatrist who testified at the punishment phase on the issue of future dangerousness could derive his expert opinion from the study of technical works, specialized education, practical experience, or a combination thereof.


OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction for capital murder from the trial court was affirmed because evidence admitted at trial was seized pursuant to a valid search warrant and any omissions in the affidavit that was used to support the search warrant were unintentional.

PRIOR HISTORY: 102nd District Court of Red River County.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant was granted automatic review of a judgment from the 102nd District Court of Red River County (Texas), which convicted appellant of capital murder pursuant to Tex. Penal Code Ann. § 19.03(a)(2) and sentenced appellant to death.


OVERVIEW: Appellant and friends kicked in the decedent's front door, robbed her, shot her in the head, and stole her car. Appellant was indicted for the offense of capital murder pursuant to Tex. Penal Code Ann. § 19.03(a)(2), alleging murder in the course of committing and attempting to commit burglary of a habitation or robbery. The court found that the trial court's error in admitting evidence of appellant's gang affiliations made no contribution to the guilty verdict or punishment and was therefore harmless error.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court that convicted appellant of capital murder and sentenced him to death. The court held that the trial court's findings were not clearly erroneous and although the trial court erred in admitting evidence of appellant's gang affiliations, it was harmless error.

Fant v. State


NO. 1047-94


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF TEXAS


931 S.W.2d 299; 1996 Tex. Crim. App. LEXIS 209



October 16, 1996, Delivered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As Corrected October 22, 1996.


PRIOR HISTORY: Petition for Discretionary Review from the Fourteenth. (HARRIS County).



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Prosecution sought review of an order of the Court of Appeals of Texas, 14th District, Houston, which reversed an order of the trial court denying defendant habeas corpus relief in action that alleged criminal prosecution was barred by double jeopardy due to prior forfeiture proceedings under Tex. Code Crim. Proc. Ann. art. 59.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was arrested and charged with possession of a controlled substance with intent to deliver. Prosecution initiated forfeiture proceedings against cash and property that were seized from defendant when he was arrested. An agreed judgment was reached regarding the forfeiture. Defendant then brought a habeas corpus challenge to his criminal indictment that was denied by the trial court. The lower court reversed the trial court and found that the purpose of Tex. Code Crim. Proc. Ann. art. 59 was to punish drug traffickers, but defendant had already been punished by the forfeiture proceedings. The court reversed the lower court and held that the forfeiture proceeding was in rem, and it was the property that was proceeded against and not the person, which made the Double Jeopardy Clause of the U.S. Const. amend V not applicable. The court held that the forfeiture of defendant's property was a civil in rem proceeding that was neither punitive nor criminal in nature. The court sustained appellee's ground for review and remanded the case to the trial court for the defendant to answer the indictment.


OUTCOME: The order of the lower court that granted habeas corpus relief to defendant was reversed and remanded because his criminal prosecution was not barred on double jeopardy grounds because of prior forfeiture proceedings.

State v. Edmond


No. 0958-95


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF TEXAS


933 S.W.2d 120; 1996 Tex. Crim. App. LEXIS 204



October 2, 1996, DELIVERED  
October 2, 1996, filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As Amended October 17, 1996.


PRIOR HISTORY: Petition for Discretionary Review from the Second Court of Appeals. 89th District Court of Wichita County.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED in part, REVERSED in part, and REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state of Texas sought discretionary review of a judgment rendered by the Second Court of Appeals (Texas) that quashed its indictment against appellee former police officer, charging official oppression under Tex. Penal Code Ann. § 39.02.


OVERVIEW: Appellee former policeman was indicted for official oppression under Tex. Penal Code Ann. § 39.02. The indictment contained four paragraphs, each alleging an alternative means of committing the offense. The court of appeals affirmed the trial court's order that quashed all four paragraphs of the indictment. Discretionary review was granted to determine whether the court of appeals erred when it held that an indictment for official oppression must specify appellee's alleged act of "mistreatment" and must describe or define "unwelcome sexual advances." The state argued that the best interpretation of § 39.02(a)(1) was that "knowledge of illegality" did not modify "mistreatment." The court disagreed and affirmed the judgment as to paragraph two because the correct way to interpret the official oppression statute was that "knowledge of illegality" modified "mistreatment." The court reversed the quashing of paragraph four of the indictment because the holding that the indictment must describe "unwelcome sexual advances" and "request for sexual favors" was in error. It was sufficient that the state elect among the alternative methods of harassment that it planned to prove at trial.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment that quashed the state's indictment as to paragraph two of the official oppression indictment because the lower courts correctly interpreted the statute as to the correct usage of "knowledge of illegality" and "mistreatment." The court reversed the quashing of paragraph four of the indictment because the lower courts erred in holding that the indictment improperly failed to define "unwelcome sexual advances."

In sum, as there were no evidentiary, substantive, or constitutional errors requiring reversal, the judgment was affirmed.


OUTCOME: Appellant's murder conviction and death sentence were affirmed because appellant failed to show reversible error regarding his confession, the conduct of the prosecution at trial, or the trial court's instructions to the jury at the guilt and penalty phases of the trial. The court examined the record and determined that any error was waived by appellant's failure to preserve issues on the record and the trial court ruled properly.

Soria v. State


NO. 69,679


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF TEXAS


933 S.W.2d 46; 1996 Tex. Crim. App. LEXIS 183



September 11, 1996, DELIVERED  
September 11, 1996, filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As Corrected September 20, 1996.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Criminal District Court No. 4 of Tarrant County.



DISPOSITION: State's motion for rehearing sustained. Judgment of the trial court affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review from Criminal District Court No. 4, Tarrant County (Texas), where he was convicted of capital murder asserting a variety of errors including procedural, evidentiary, constitutional, and trial errors, along with jury and punishment phase issues. The state filed a motion for rehearing to a reversal of the death penalty, contending the evidence was legally sufficient to support the death sentence finding.


OVERVIEW: Appellant pursued and executed a scheme to kill a man and deprive him of his vehicle. Appellant was convicted of capital murder and sentenced to death. Subsequently, the death sentence was reversed but the conviction was affirmed. Appellant sought review regarding the conviction, asserting errors and the state filed a motion for rehearing seeking reimposition of the death penalty, contending the evidence was legally sufficient to support the findings as to the death sentence. On review, the court examined in detail the record regarding voir dire and found that there was no reversible error in the selection of the jury as contended by appellant and, if there was any error, it was harmless. The court found that the trial court did not abuse its discretion regarding the admission of evidence in the way of testimony or otherwise. The court also found that there was no reversible error with reference to the jury instructions. However, the court was of the opinion that appellant's actions constituted a rehearsed and planned killing and the evidence was sufficient to support the jury's affirmative findings as to the death penalty and re-imposed the death sentence accordingly.


OUTCOME: The capital murder judgment was affirmed because there was no reversible error in the selection of the jury, the admission of evidence in the way of testimony or otherwise, or the jury instructions. However, the state's motion for rehearing regarding the death penalty was sustained and the death sentence was re-imposed because evidence that the killing was planned was sufficient to support the jury's affirmative findings.

 

177 of 1793 DOCUMENTS


Long v. Texas


NO. 803-95


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF TEXAS


931 S.W.2d 285; 1996 Tex. Crim. App. LEXIS 184



September 11, 1996, DELIVERED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Petition for Discretionary Review from the Third Court of Appeals. County Court at Law No. 3 of Travis County.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeals is reversed, and the case is remanded to the trial court to enter an order dismissing the prosecution.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant filed a petition for discretionary review from the Third Court of Appeals, County Court at Law No. 3 of Travis County (Texas), where appellant was convicted under the "stalking" provision of the 1993 harassment statute pursuant to Tex. Penal Code Ann. § 42.07(a)(7) (1993). Appellant asserted that the stalking provision was unconstitutionally vague on its face and as applied to his conduct.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted under the "stalking" provision of the harassment statute, Tex. Penal Code Ann. § 42.07(a)(7) (1993). Thereafter, appellant filed a petition for discretionary review contending that the stalking provision was unconstitutionally vague on its face and as applied to his conduct. On review, the court noted that criminal laws were to sufficiently clear in order to be upheld and, if a statute was capable of reaching U.S. Const. amend. I freedoms, the doctrine of vagueness demanded a greater degree of specificity than in other contexts. The court examined the statute in detail and the wording "intent to harass, annoy, alarm, abuse, torment, or embarrass" as applied to appellant's situation. The court noted that the statute did not limit the vagueness of the conduct by linking it to more specific conduct through a continuity of purpose requirement. The court found that a savings clause as to affirmative defense or constitutionally protected activity was not sufficient. The court concluded that the statute was vague and this rendered the entire statute unconstitutional. Therefore, the judgment was reversed and the case was remanded to the trial court for dismissal.


OUTCOME: Appellant's conviction for violation of the stalking statute was reversed and the case was remanded to the trial court to enter an order dismissing the prosecution because the statute was vague and this rendered the entire statute unconstitutional. The court found that the implicit U.S. Const. amend. I freedoms and the statutory language could not be reconciled without further clarification of the statute.

Vaughn v. State


NO. 234-95


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF TEXAS


931 S.W.2d 564; 1996 Tex. Crim. App. LEXIS 186



September 11, 1996, Delivered


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Petition for Discretionary Review from the First Court of Appeals. 180th District Court of Harris County.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a petition for discretionary review from an order of the First Court of Appeals, 180 District Court of Harris County (Texas), affirming her conviction in the trial court, for the felony offense of aggravated assault under Tex. Penal Code Ann. § 22.02.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of the felony offense of aggravated assault. After the jury found defendant guilty, it assessed her punishment at a six-year term, probated, and a fine. Defendant challenged the conviction to the court of appeals on grounds that she was denied her right to reasonably effective assistance of counsel at the punishment phase of trial under Tex. Const. art. I, § 10. The court of appeals affirmed the conviction. Defendant filed a petition for discretionary review. Defendant argued that her trial counsel was ineffective for not objecting to questions posed to her at the sentencing phase of the trial regarding her guilt or innocence. The court affirmed, holding that there was no caselaw dispositive of, or even addressing, the issue. That defendant was put in a position where she potentially was exposed to answer perjury charges if she admitted her guilt did not clearly violate due process or her right against self-incrimination. The court held that defendant's predicament was a logical and constitutional result of a series of choices made in her defense.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment affirming appellant's conviction of the felony of aggravated assault and the finding that appellant's counsel was not ineffective. The court held that appellant's ineffective assistance of counsel claim could not be based on an alleged error of counsel when the caselaw evaluating counsel's actions and decisions was nonexistent or not definitive.

 

179 of 1793 DOCUMENTS


Morris v. State


NO. 71,799


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF TEXAS


940 S.W.2d 610; 1996 Tex. Crim. App. LEXIS 187



September 11, 1996, Delivered  
September 11, 1996, filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As Corrected September 11, 1996. Second Correction September 18, 1996. Third Correction July 7, 1997. Certiorari Denied June 16, 1997, Reported at: 1997 U.S. LEXIS 3791.
Rehearing denied by, 01/08/1997
Writ of certiorari denied Morris v. Texas, 520 U.S. 1278, 117 S. Ct. 2461, 138 L. Ed. 2d 218, 1997 U.S. LEXIS 3791 (1997)
Writ of habeas corpus dismissed, Motion denied by Ex parte Morris, 2009 Tex. Crim. App. Unpub. LEXIS 153 (2009)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a conviction for capital murder under Tex. Penal Code Ann. § 19.03(a)(2) in the trial court (Texas). He disputed the state's striking of a black juror, the exclusion from the jury of issues as to parole, the exclusion of mitigating evidence of comparative sentencing and the sufficiency of the evidence on mitigation, and the constitutionality of various aspects of the trial, including the application of the death penalty.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of capital murder, and he appealed on many grounds. The court held that the trial judge's approval of the state's exclusion of a black juror during voir dire was not clearly erroneous. Defendant could not put issues of parole before the jury, whether or not the issues bore on his future dangerousness, because parole was not an issue for the jury's consideration in a capital murder case. Its exclusion was not unconstitutional. Evidence of sentences received by other defendants in similar circumstances was not admissible as mitigating evidence. The court would not review the mitigating evidence for sufficiency, because the weighing of that evidence was a matter for subjective determination by each juror. The mitigation issues, issues concerning the burdens of proof, and the death penalty were constitutional under U.S. Const. amend. VIII and XIV. A United States Supreme Court decision in a civil case did not constitutionally require a "proportionality review" of the defendant's sentence. There was no authority for defendant's argument that the state constitution should be interpreted more broadly than the United States constitution. The court affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed. The trial court's decision on the juror was not clearly erroneous. Parole was not an issue for the jury in a capital murder case, and its exclusion was not unconstitutional. Evidence of other defendants' sentences was not a mitigating circumstance. The issue of the sufficiency of mitigating evidence was not reviewable by the court. None of the defendant's many claims of unconstitutionality had merit.

Anderson v. State


NO. 71,808


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF TEXAS


932 S.W.2d 502; 1996 Tex. Crim. App. LEXIS 193



September 11, 1996, DELIVERED  
September 11, 1996, filed


NOTICE:   [**1]  PUBLISHED IN PART


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected November 21, 1996. Certiorari Denied June 27, 1997, Reported at: 1997 U.S. LEXIS 4115.
Rehearing denied by, 10/30/1996
Writ of certiorari denied Anderson v. Texas, 521 U.S. 1122, 138 L. Ed. 2d 1019, 117 S. Ct. 2517, 1997 U.S. LEXIS 4115 (1997)
Habeas corpus proceeding at, Summary judgment denied by Anderson v. Johnson, 2001 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 15408 (N.D. Tex., Sept. 27, 2001)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from 108th District Court of Potter.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant was convicted of murder from the District Court of Potter County, Texas, during the course of an aggravated sexual assault and aggravated kidnapping and sentenced to death. Appellant argued that he was improperly seized by the police.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of murder during the course of aggravated sexual assault and aggravated kidnapping and sentenced to death. Appellant was observed dumping an ice box that contained the body of a child. Appellant argued that the pretrial identifications were the fruits of an illegal arrest in violation of U. S. Const. amend. IV. A person has been seized within the meaning of U. S. Const. amend. IV only if, in view of all the circumstances surrounding the incident, a reasonable person would have believed that he was not free to leave. The court has held that a person who voluntarily accompanied investigating police officers to a certain location knowing that he was a suspect had not been seized for U. S. Const. amend. IV purposes. The judgment was affirmed because although the officer would have detained appellant if he had refused to return to the witness' location, the office never communicated this intent.


OUTCOME: Appellant's murder conviction and his death sentence were affirmed because the evidence showed that appellant agreed to accompany the police officer back to the crime scene in hopes of exonerating himself and therefore, he was not illegally seized.

 

181 of 1793 DOCUMENTS


State v. Daugherty


NO. 924-94


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF TEXAS


931 S.W.2d 268; 1996 Tex. Crim. App. LEXIS 88



June 26, 1996, Delivered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Counsel Amended December 27, 1996.


PRIOR HISTORY: Petition for Discretionary Review from the Second Court of Appeals. Criminal District Court No. 3 Tarrant County.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the court of appeals vacated and remanded for further proceedings pursuant to Rule 81(b)(2).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellee state filed a petition for discretionary review of a judgment from the Second Court of Appeals (Texas) which declined to recognize the inevitable discovery doctrine to the statutory exclusionary rule, Tex. Code Crim. Proc. Ann. art. 38.23(a). Appellee argued that the doctrine should be adopted when the causal connection between illegal conduct and evidence was broken and when the U.S. Supreme Court had approved the doctrine.


OVERVIEW: The court affirmed the holding of a judgment that the inevitable discovery doctrine did not apply to Texas's exclusionary rule, Tex. Code Crim. Proc. Ann. art. 38.23(a), but vacated and remanded for a analysis under Tex. R. App. P. 81(b)(2). The court rejected the reasoning of the dissent that the inevitable discovery doctrine, like the attenuation of taint doctrine, served to legally break the causal connection between the illegal conduct and the acquisition of evidence. The court held that the plain language of the statute did not accommodate the inevitable discovery doctrine because once the illegality, and its causal connection, were established, the evidence must be excluded. The court explained that the inevitable discovery doctrine was a court-created doctrine to the federal exclusionary rule, not an exception to U.S Const. amend. IV, and that the court was obligated to determine the Texas legislature's intent. The court did find, however, that once the appellate court determined that the evidence should have been suppressed, it should have conducted a harmless error analysis, pursuant to Tex. R. App. P. 81(b)(2). The court remanded for that analysis.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the holding of the judgment that the inevitable discovery doctrine did not apply to the Texas exclusionary rule under the plain language of the statute. However, the court agreed that once the appellate court determined the evidence should have been suppressed, it should have conducted a harmless error analysis. Therefore, the court vacated and remanded for that analysis.

Ramos v. State


NO. 71,714


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF TEXAS

SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by, 09/11/1996

Decision reached on appeal by Ramos v. Cockrell, 32 Fed. Appx. 126, 2002 U.S. App. LEXIS 3916 (5th Cir. Tex., 2002)

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Criminal appellant sought review of a judgment from the 93rd District Court of Appeal from Hidalgo County (Texas) that convicted him of capital murder attacking the validity of the search warrants.


OVERVIEW: Criminal appellant complained that law enforcement officials illegally obtained evidence. A search warrant, based upon affidavit, was issued to search appellant's home. The warrant authorized a search for blood, clothing containing blood, a weapon and/or object used in the commission of a murder, and any and all evidence relating to a homicide and/or a kidnapping. On appeal, the court found that the magistrate had sufficient evidence of the alleged crimes to issue the warrant. OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment convicting criminal appellant. The magistrate had sufficient evidence of the alleged crimes to issue the warrant, the second search warrant was valid insofar as it authorized a search for the victims' bodies, and there was no federal due process nor equal protection violations.

HOWARD v. STATE


No. 71,739


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF TEXAS


941 S.W.2d 102; 1996 Tex. Crim. App. LEXIS 92



June 19, 1996, DELIVERED  
June 19, 1996, filed


PRIOR HISTORY:   [**1] Appeal from the 24th District Court of Jackson County.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment from the 24th District Court of Jackson County (Texas), which convicted him of capital murder and sentenced him to death. Defendant argued 34 points of error including errors with respect to the jury selection process, the giving of an Allen charge, and upon jury deadlock, the trial court's refusal to terminate the punishment phase to impose a life sentence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of capital murder of a state trooper. The jury found defendant to be a future danger and declined to find mitigating circumstances under Tex. Code Crim. Proc. Ann. arts. 37.071, § 2(b) and 37.071, § 2(e). Accordingly, the trial court sentenced defendant to death. On appeal, defendant argued 34 points of error including errors involving jury selection, an Allen charge, and the refusal to terminate the punishment phase pursuant to jury deadlock to impose a life sentence. The reviewing court found no error. The properly disqualified jurors indicated either that they would add legal elements to the state's burden at trial or that they would never impose a death penalty. There was no abuse of discretion in denying termination of the punishment phase in light of the appearance of deliberative breakthrough. The Allen charge was not coercive because the trial court did not attempt to identify the minority jurors. The mere presence of twenty uniformed officers at the back of the courtroom during the punishment phase, without more, did not provide a basis for a finding of inherent prejudice resulting in a lack of due process. Therefore, the judgment was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for capital murder and the imposition of the death penalty. The court found no merit in defendant's allegations of error. The jury selection process was proper, the giving of the Allen charge was noncoercive, and the refusal to terminate the punishment phase was not an abuse of discretion in light of the appearance of progress in the jury's deliberative process.

Goff v. State


No. 71,404


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF TEXAS


931 S.W.2d 537; 1996 Tex. Crim. App. LEXIS 68



May 22, 1996, DELIVERED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Certiorari Denied April 14, 1997, Reported at: 1997 U.S. LEXIS 2302.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from 371st District Court of TARRANT County.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the judgment of the 371st District Court of Tarrant County (Texas), which convicted him of capital murder and sentenced him to death. Among the 23 points of error raised, defendant argued that the trial court had erred in submitting an instruction on the law of the parties in a guilt/innocence charge.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted by a jury in the court below of the offense of capital murder, namely murder in the course of kidnapping or burglary under Tex. Penal Code Ann. § 19.03(a)(2). After the jury affirmatively answered the submitted special issues, the trial court sentenced defendant to death. The trial court's judgment was affirmed on appeal despite 23 points of error raised by defendant on appeal. Contrary to defendant's contention, the trial court was not found to have erred in submitting an instruction on the law of parties in the guilt/innocence charge because there was evidence to support the inference that defendant acted as a party to the offense. Moreover, the court found that the State had not impermissibly commented on defendant's failure to testify because the comments at issue merely constituted a proper summation of the evidence. Finally, the trial court did not commit error when it refused to admit evidence concerning the victim's homosexuality at punishment because the victim's homosexuality was not relevant as a mitigating circumstance to defendant's background, character, or the circumstances of the crime.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the court below, which convicted defendant of capital murder and sentenced him to death, was affirmed on appeal after all 23 points of error raised by defendant were overruled.

Martinez v. State


NO. 71,818


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF TEXAS


924 S.W.2d 693; 1996 Tex. Crim. App. LEXIS 70



May 22, 1996, Delivered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As Corrected August 23, 1996. Second Correction October 25, 1996.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from 347th District Court of NUECES County.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of his death sentence for capital murder from 347th District Court of Nueces County (Texas). Appellant asserted there was insufficient evidence to support the jury's affirmative answer to special issue on future dangerousness, pursuant to Tex. Code Crim. Proc. Ann. art. 37.071 § 2(b) and that trial court erred in refusing to give a Simmons instruction on parole eligibility or define the word "militates."


OVERVIEW: The court affirmed appellant's conviction for capital murder and his death sentence. Appellant was drinking with friends and decided to rob a convenience store using a knife. Appellant threatened the clerk, forced him to lie on the ground, then stabbed him several times in the back and the neck. Appellee state did not put on any additional evidence at sentencing and appellant put on one character witness. The court held that the evidence was sufficient for the jury to affirmatively answer the special issue on future dangerousness, pursuant to Tex. Code Crim. Proc. Ann. art. 37.071 § 2(b). The court held that the brutal facts of the stabbing itself, including that the majority of the knife thrusts were into the back of an already fallen victim; the conflicting testimony as to why appellant decided to commit a robbery; the number of lies that he told the police; and his apparent disregard for the law and authority supported the finding. The court held the trial court did not err in refusing to give a Simmons instruction on appellant's parole eligibility and in refusing to define the word "militates" in the punishment charge.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's death sentence. The court held that the finding of future dangerousness was supported by the brutal facts of the stabbing alone, in which blows were inflicted on fallen victim, and when appellant lied to police, gave no reason for his conduct, and had a history of disregard for authority. The court held that trial court did not err in refusing parole eligibility instruction or to define the word "militates."

State v. Adams


NOS. 1177-93 & 1178-93


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF TEXAS


930 S.W.2d 88; 1996 Tex. Crim. App. LEXIS 52



April 24, 1996, Delivered


PRIOR HISTORY:   [**1] Petition for Discretionary Review from the First Court of Appeals. 262nd District Court of Harris County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: State sought review of the decision of the First Court of Appeals (Texas), that found that it did not have authority to interfere with an abatement order issued by a court of appeals with equal jurisdiction when state filed a motion to compel trial court to vacate its order granting appellants' motions for new trials after their convictions for engaging in organized criminal activity.


OVERVIEW: Appellants were convicted of engaging in organized crime. After the trial court denied their motions for new trials, they filed notices of appeal with the appellate court. While those appeals were pending, they filed amended motions for new trials with the trial court. The state objected arguing that the trial court no longer had jurisdiction. The appellate court concluded that the trial court did not have jurisdiction, but vacated the orders and abated the appeals, thereby creating jurisdiction for the trial court. The trial court granted the motions for new trials, and the state properly sought review with a different appellate court. That appellate court found that it did not have authority to interfere with the abatement order of the first appellate court because it had equal jurisdiction with that court. The state brought a petition for review. The court found that the state's argument was that the trial court did not have jurisdiction to hear the motions for new trials, not whether or not the abatement order was proper. The court found that the second appellate court had the authority to address this issue and that it erred in failing to do so. It reversed and remanded.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded the cause finding that the appellate court, in concluding that it did not have authority to interfere with an abatement order, failed to address to issue presented by the state which was whether the trial court had lawful jurisdiction to hear an out-of-time motion for a new trial.

Smith v. State


NO. 71,794


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF TEXAS

PRIOR HISTORY: 174th District Court of Harris County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant condemned challenged a decision of the 174th District Court of Harris County (Texas), which found him guilty of capital murder in violation of Tex. Penal Code Ann. § 19.03(a)(2). Appellant was sentenced to death pursuant to Tex. Code Crim. Proc. art. 37.071 §§ 2(b) and (e).


OVERVIEW: Appellant condemned challenged his conviction and death sentence for capital murder in violation of Tex. Penal Code Ann. § 19.03(a)(2). The court held that aggravated robbery and murder was a lesser included offense, but intent to kill following the robbery was supported by evidence of death by contact wound and the heavy pull of the gun's trigger; also, a guilty finding only as to murder required no evidence of relationship to robbery, which was impossible. The court held that the trial court erred in admitting victim impact evidence regarding the victim's good works because it was irrelevant and unnecessary for rebuttal, but found the error harmless in the face of other adverse evidence. The court found no error in the denial of appellant's motion for mistrial because the state referred to something "only appellant knows," as it was cured by instruction to disregard. The court held that appellant's request to change a peremptory challenge was tantamount to requesting another, so its denial was within the trial court's discretion.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment and death sentence in appellant condemned's challenge although it ruled error in admission of victim impact testimony during the punishment stage, holding that the error was harmless in comparison to overwhelming evidence of appellant's culpability.

Ford v. State


No. 71,760


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF TEXAS


919 S.W.2d 107; 1996 Tex. Crim. App. LEXIS 22



February 21, 1996, Delivered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As Corrected February 23, 1996. Second Correction May 24, 1996.
Rehearing denied by, 04/03/1996

PRIOR HISTORY: 346th District Court of El Paso County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review from the 346th District Court of El Paso County (Texas) which convicted him of three counts of capital murder and three counts of attempted capital murder and sentenced him to death. Appellant contended that the evidence was insufficient to support the jury's affirmative answer during the punishment phase of the case.


OVERVIEW: Appellant and an accomplice forced their way into the home of a mother with three children, robbed them, and shot them. Appellant was convicted of three counts of capital murder and three counts of attempted capital murder and was sentenced to death. Appellant contended that the victim impact evidence during the punishment phase was insufficient to support the jury's answers. The court determined that evidence of appellant's lack of remorse, refusal to accept responsibility for his actions, and attempt to blame others were sufficient for the jury to rationally determine that there was a probability of appellant's future dangerousness. Although victim impact evidence did not appear in rules of evidence, the court held that the state was allowed to admit evidence about the victim and the impact of the murder on the victim's family as being relevant to whether or not death should be imposed. The court held that the lower court did not abuse its discretion in allowing victim impact evidence because its probative value was not substantially outweighed by the danger of unfair prejudice towards appellant. The court affirmed the judgment of the lower court.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the lower court, which convicted appellant defendant of three counts of capital murder and three counts of attempted capital murder and sentenced him to death. The court held that, during the punishment phase, the state was allowed to admit victim impact evidence to the jury because its probative value was not substantially outweighed by any danger of unfair prejudice towards appellant.

Ransom v. State


NO. 71,633


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF TEXAS


920 S.W.2d 288; 1996 Tex. Crim. App. LEXIS 23



February 21, 1996, DELIVERED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As Amended. As Corrected March 27, 1996. Second Correction April 2, 1996. 920 S.W.2d 288 at 297.


PRIOR HISTORY: Criminal District Court No. 1 of Tarrant County.

Original Opinion of June 15, 1994, Reported at: 1994 Tex. Crim. App. LEXIS 79.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant's conviction and sentence from the Criminal District Court No. 1 of Tarrant County (Texas) was vacated and the cause was remanded for a new trial. The State filed a motion for rehearing.


OVERVIEW: The court granted rehearing of its prior opinion that reversed and remanded the case for a new trial. After rehearing, the court affirmed the conviction, but vacated the sentence and remanded the matter for a new sentencing hearing because errors affecting punishment only required a new hearing on punishment and defendant produced no evidence which showed the error produced a jury biased against him on the issue of guilt. The court held it was not necessary to change venue based on prejudicial publicity because the jury had been selected and instructed to avoid media coverage when it occurred, and the juror who was exposed was excused. The court held that evidence of defendant's attack on the attorneys was admissible during the guilt phases to show consciousness of guilt, and evidence of the extraneous offense of stealing the gun was admissible to connect defendant to the murder. It also held that failure to notify of prosecution deal with accomplice prior to trial did not require exclusion of testimony because the deal was made on the day the accomplice testified, the details were presented to the jury, and defense attorney cross-examined him regarding the deal.


OUTCOME: Upon rehearing, the court affirmed the conviction but vacated the sentence and remanded the cause for a new hearing on punishment because the voir dire error regarded a subject that the jury would only consider during the punishment phase and affected only the punishment phase, and defendant produced no evidence showing the error produced a jury biased against him on the issue of guilt.

Clewis v. State


NO. 450-94


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF TEXAS


922 S.W.2d 126; 1996 Tex. Crim. App. LEXIS 11



January 31, 1996, Delivered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As Corrected February 5, 1996. As Amended February 12, 1996. Second Correction July 25, 1996.


PRIOR HISTORY: Petition for Discretionary Review from the Fifth Court of Appeals. Criminal District Court No. 2 of Dallas Co.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant filed a Petition for Discretionary Review after the Fifth Court of Appeals, Criminal District Court No. 2 of Dallas County (Texas), affirmed his conviction for burglary of a building.


OVERVIEW: Appellant claimed the court of appeals erred in refusing to review the evidence to determine whether it was factually sufficient to sustain his conviction for burglary of a building. At issue was the proper standard of review for factual sufficiency of the elements of the offense in the courts of appeals. The court held that the standard for legal sufficiency was whether, after viewing the evidence in the light most favorable to the prosecution, any rational trier of fact could have found the essential elements of the crime beyond a reasonable doubt. As the standard of review of factual sufficiency the court held that the appellate court must view all the evidence without the prism of in the light most favorable to the prosecution and set aside the verdict only if it is so contrary to the overwhelming weight of the evidence as to be clearly wrong and unjust. The court vacated the judgment of the lower court and remanded for further proceedings.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the judgment of the court of appeals affirming appellant's conviction and remanded the case for further proceedings to determine, when applying the proper standard of review, if the evidence was factually sufficient to convict appellant of burglary.

 

193 of 1793 DOCUMENTS


Hill v. State


No. 1179-94 & 1180-94


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF TEXAS


913 S.W.2d 581; 1996 Tex. Crim. App. LEXIS 2



January 10, 1996, DELIVERED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As Amended March 11, 1996.


PRIOR HISTORY: Petition for Discretionary Review from the Second Court of Appeals. (Tarrant County).



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the court of appeals is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants petitioned for discretionary review from the Second Court of Appeals, Tarrant County (Texas), decision which affirmed their convictions for the offense of injury to a child and which also affirmed the finding that the chains, belts, and locks that were used to prevent their child from obtaining food constituted dangerous weapons.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, husband and wife, were charged by separate indictments with the offense of injury to a child. Appellee state filed notice of its intent to seek an affirmative finding that a deadly weapon was used in a manner that caused their child's death. The chains, belts, and locks that were used to prevent their child from obtaining food, resulting in the child dying of starvation, were identified by appellee as the deadly weapons. Testimony at trial showed the child was chained for approximately 18 months prior to his death. The jury found appellants guilty of injury to a child by omission and also found that appellants used a deadly weapon in the commission of the offense. Appellant argued that that chains, belts, and locks were not deadly weapons per se as defined under Tex. Penal Code Ann. § 1.07(a)(17)(A). On review, the court affirmed the convictions. The court held that items that are not deadly weapons per se under § 1.07(a)(17)(A) could be found to be deadly weapons by nature of their use or intended use, and that appellee introduced evidence that the restraints were used to cause serious bodily injury or death.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the convictions and the jury finding that a deadly weapon was used in the commission of the offense, holding that even though chains, belts, and locks were not deadly weapons per se as defined under the applicable statute, such items could be found to be deadly weapons by the nature of their use or intended use, and that appellee state introduced evidence that the restraints were used to cause serious bodily injury or death.

 

194 of 1793 DOCUMENTS


Brimage v. State


No. 70,105


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF TEXAS


918 S.W.2d 466; 1996 Tex. Crim. App. LEXIS 5



January 10, 1996, DELIVERED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As Corrected January 24, 1996. As Amended February 7, 1996.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal From The 105th District Court (Kleberg County).

Original Opinion of September 21, 1994, Reported at: 1994 Tex. Crim. App. LEXIS 96.



DISPOSITION: Having found no reversible error, we affirm the judgment of the trial court.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Criminal appellant sought review of a judgment from the 105th District Court, Kleberg County (Texas), convicting him of capital murder pursuant to Tex. Penal Code Ann. § 19.03(a)(2) (1985).


OVERVIEW: Criminal appellant was convicted of capital murder. He contended that the trial court erred in admitting evidence and the fruits of an illegal search of his residence, in ordering a transfer of venue, in failing to suppress the confession, in allowing prejudicial opinion testimony, and in instructing the jury. The court concluded that the evidence seized from appellant's residence during the search for complainant or potential additional victims was lawfully seized under the "emergency doctrine," so the trial court did not abuse its discretion in ruling the evidence was admissible at trial. Appellant failed to show any abuse of discretion by the trial court, and the record amply supported its findings. Appellant failed to show he was prejudiced by not being brought before a magistrate prior to being interviewed by investigator. Appellant also failed to present evidence of any police misconduct or coercion relating to his confession that supported his claim of involuntariness. The trial court did not err in allowing the opinion testimony and in charging the jury. Therefore, the trial court's judgment was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed criminal appellant's conviction of capital murder. The evidence seized from appellant's residence during the search for complainant or potential additional victims was lawfully seized under the "emergency doctrine," so the trial court did not abuse its discretion in ruling the evidence was admissible at trial.

Casarez v. State


NO. 1114-93


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF TEXAS


913 S.W.2d 468; 1995 Tex. Crim. App. LEXIS 132



December 13, 1995, DELIVERED  
December 13, 1995, filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] 913 S.W.2d 468 at 492. As Corrected April 7, 1997.


PRIOR HISTORY: Petition for Discretionary Review from the Second Court of Appeals. 371st District Court of Tarrant County.

Original Opinion of December 14, 1994, Reported at: 1994 Tex. App. LEXIS 140.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant petitioned for discretionary review from the Second Court of Appeals. 371st District Court of Tarrant County (Texas), which affirmed his conviction because it found that state could exercise peremptory challenges based on religion.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of aggravated sexual battery. He appealed, arguing the equal protection rule forbid peremptory challenged motivated by religious prejudice. The conviction was affirmed and appellant sought discretionary review. The court distinguished between unlawful peremptory challenges based on sex or race and those based on religion. The exercise of peremptory challenges against individual veniremembers was a government practice subject to equal protection rules. Thus, a prospective juror could not be excluded on a basis offensive to the Constitution. However, the court held that discrimination on the basis of personal belief was a proper consideration in jury selection in determining suitability for jury service. The court held that the interest served by the system of peremptory challenges were sufficiently great to justify state implementation of choices made by litigants to exclude persons from service on juries in individual cases on the basis of their religious affiliation.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's conviction because it found no error in the state using its peremptory challenges to exclude prospective jurors on the basis of their religious beliefs.

Lawton v. State


NO. 71,759


COURT OF CRIMINAL APPEALS OF TEXAS


913 S.W.2d 542; 1995 Tex. Crim. App. LEXIS 127



December 6, 1995, DELIVERED  
December 6, 1995, filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As Corrected December 12, 1995.
Rehearing denied by, 01/17/1996
Writ of certiorari denied Lawton v. Texas, 519 U.S. 826, 117 S. Ct. 88, 136 L. Ed. 2d 44, 1996 U.S. LEXIS 4877 (1996)
Writ of habeas corpus denied Lawton v. Johnson, 211 F.3d 125, 2000 U.S. App. LEXIS 5691 (5th Cir. Tex., 2000)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from 114th District Court of Smith County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a judgment of the 114th District Court of Smith County (Texas) convicting him of murder and imposing a sentence of death, arguing that the court committed several evidentiary and procedural errors during the course of his trial.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was tried and convicted of capital murder. The murder was committed in the course robbing automobiles in the pre-dawn hours of Christmas eve. Appellant was sentenced to death as mandated by Tex. Code Crim. Proc. Ann. art. 37.071 § 2(g). Appellant raised 28 points of error on appeal. The court affirmed appellant's conviction because the evidence indicated that in a well planned spree of vehicle burglaries, appellant stood as an armed guard to protect those committing the burglaries. Appellant assured his accomplices that he was willing to kill anyone who attempted to interrupt their crime spree, and when the victim attempted to do so, appellant shot him without warning. Witness testimony established that appellant was a continuing threat to society, and had a bad reputation in the community. The evidence was sufficient to rationally support the jury's verdict.


OUTCOME: Appellant's conviction for capital murder and death sentence were affirmed because the evidence was sufficient to rationally support the jury's verdict that he shot the victim in the course of robbing automobiles in the pre-dawn hours of Christmas eve without warning.
